

2011 WL 1570476 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TATIANA VASOUEZ
File: AXXX XX9 947 - Detroit, MI
March 31, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeremy Santoro
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopen
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on September 5, 2008. We remanded the record to the Immigration Judge to reevaluate the respondent’s motion to reopen, which was filed following an in absentia order of removal. On October 1, 2009, the Immigration Judge denied the motion, and the respondent filed this appeal. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard, and review all other issues, including whether the parties have met their burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
In our remand order, we agreed with the respondent’s assertion that prior counsel’s failure to file an entry of appearance, and failure to notify the court of a change of address on behalf of the respondent, are facts that if established, would lead to a finding that the respondent was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We pointed out that the record is replete with evidence of malfeasance by the attorney in question, which could contribute to the respondent’s credibility. We remanded the record for the Immigration Judge to assess whether ineffective assistance of counsel prevented the respondent from receiving notice of her hearing.
 
On remand, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent did not prove that counsel in question was actually retained as her attorney for these removal proceedings or that she in fact did not receive her Notice to Appear and Notice of Hearing. The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge committed clear error in her factual findings.
 
As acknowledged by the Immigration Judge, the respondent complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to support her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. There is also an abundance of evidence that the counsel in question misrepresented his clients in various immigration matters. The outstanding issue is whether in fact the respondent had retained this counsel to represent her in removal proceedings. We find that the evidence of record supports the respondent’s contention that this was the situation; the Immigration Judge’s finding otherwise is clearly erroneous. The respondent submitted a detailed statement explaining her relationship with former counsel, payment receipts and other documentation, and an abundance of evidence regarding the attorney’s malfeasance in other cases. Further, the prior attorney failed to respond to the respondent’s allegations against him, and did not appear in court pursuant to the subpoena issued for him by the Immigration Judge (see Tr.at 13,20-21). Under the totality of these circumstances, we find the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent did not establish that she had engaged former counsel to represent her in removal proceedings to be clearly erroneous.
 
*2 The respondent has otherwise met her burden to show ineffective assistance of counsel which resulted in prior counsel’s failure to register any address changes with the Immigration Court. The respondent can therefore not be charged with receiving proper notice of her hearing. We will accordingly vacate the in absentia order and remand the record for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The in absentia order of removal is vacated, the removal proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Ellen Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2011 WL 1570476 (BIA)

2011 WL 1373400 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CECIL LEE WILLIAMS
File: AXXX XX4 048 - Lumpkin, GA
March 29, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
The Board issued a final order on January 7, 2011, concerning the respondent, who is from Panama. A timely motion to reopen was filed with the Board on January 28, 2011. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The Board on January 7, 2011, affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable as an aggravated felon and ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction. That is, on January 22, 2008, the respondent was convicted of conspiracy to distribute and possess with intent to distribute 500 grams or more of a cocaine mixture, in violation of federal law. The Board also rejected the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his former attorney Glen Fogle, as the respondent failed to comply with the procedural requirements for making such a claim, and failed to show prejudice.
 
The respondent in his pending motion asserts that he has now met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
As before, however, the respondent fails to show prejudice stemming from the claimed actions of prior counsel. Dakane v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); Matter of Assaad, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra. “Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different.” Dakane v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra.
 
The respondent faults Fogle for not telling the Immigration Judge about “the positive aspects of my life and the ties that I have here in the United States” (Respondent’s Aff). However, having been convicted of an aggravated felony, the respondent is not eligible for relief from removal for this reason, and Fogle committed no error in conceding this at the removal hearing (Tr. at 7).
 
The respondent also argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his criminal defense attorney, and has filed a motion to vacate the conviction. This does not change the finality of the respondent’s conviction for immigration purposes, unless and until it is overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 156-67 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996).
 
*2 The pending motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The pending motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2011 WL 1373397 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERARDO COVARRUBIAS A.K.A. GERARDO COVARRUBIAS GOMEZ
File: AXXX XX8 254 - Houston, TX
March 29, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laura Sanchez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION:
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen alleging, inter alia, that his prior counsel was ineffective because he did not renew the application for adjustment of status that was based on an approved employment visa petition.
 
Even assuming that the respondent has a right to effective assistance of counsel to pursue discretionary relief such as adjustment of status, he has not demonstrated that ineffective assistance has caused him prejudice in pursuing that relief. See Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605 (5th Cir. 2006) (rejecting claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based on failure to pursue a discretionary waiver of relief from removal); Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471 (5th Cir. 2004) (“because Assaad’s attorney’s allegedly deficient performance merely restricted his chances of receiving a discretionary waiver of his removability, we conclude that he has not alleged a violation of his due process rights”); see also Altamiro-Lopez v. Gonzales, 435 F.3d 547, 550 (5th Cir. 2006) (the denial for discretionary relief does not amount to a deprivation of a liberty interest).
 
As we stated in our last order, neither the Immigration Judge nor the Board may adjudicate the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because he is an arriving alien and he does not fall within the limited exception in 8 C.F.R. § § 245.2(a)(1)(ii), 1245.2(a)(1)(ii). The respondent’s argument to the contrary is not supported by the facts. The respondent was paroled into the United States on January 12, 1997, at which time neither his family-based nor his employment-based adjustment of status applications were pending (I.J. at 3). The only evidence of an employment-based adjustment application provided by the respondent is a copy of the one that was filed on December 21, 1999, based on a skilled worker petition with a priority date of August 29, 1997(MTR at 2, Tabs D, E).¿FN1º
 
We reiterate, since counsel still seems to have a different understanding notwithstanding our last decision, that removal proceedings-even after a final removal order has been entered-do not foreclose an arriving alien from an opportunity to apply for adjustment of status with U.S. Customs and Immigration Services (“USCIS”), which is also the appropriate forum for requesting a stay of the removal order pending its adjudication by USCIS. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 107 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 With regard to the respondent’s argument that his prior counsel caused him to abandon his application for cancellation of removal, even if such an argument implicated a due process violation in the Fifth Circuit, he has neither argued nor provided evidence of specific hardships to a qualifying relative that would have been presented in support of such an application. See Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, supra; Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (requiring a showing of prejudice in order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
We will, however, reopen the proceedings for the limited purpose of reinstating the 60-day voluntary departure period granted by the Immigration Judge on January 29, 2009.¿FN2º Our October 14, 2010, decision dismissing the respondent’s appeal otherwise remains undisturbed. Accordingly, we enter the following orders.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted for the limited purpose of reinstating the period of voluntary departure granted by the Immigration Judge.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen for purposes of pursuing adjustment of status is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
*3 WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(I).
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not dispute that the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction over his family-based adjustment application.

2

The respondent has provided evidence that the voluntary departure bar was timely paid. Cf. Matter of Gamero, 25 I&N Dec. 164 (BIA 2010) (where the case was remanded to the Immigration Judge to issue a new voluntary departure order in a situation where no evidence that the bond was paid was provided to the Board, but it did not appear that the proper voluntary departure warnings were given).

2011 WL 1373408 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUPERTO TELLEZ-MEZA
File: AXXX XX9 217 - Seattle, WA
March 28, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Debra A. Morales, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Love
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on September 3, 2010, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance of the proceedings, and to grant the Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS’s) motion to terminate proceedings. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings, claiming ineffective assistance from his former counsel. The DHS opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
At his hearing, the respondent was found removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act. In his motion, the respondent principally argues that his prior attorneys, Mr. Antonio Salazar, Esquire and Mr. Andrew Oliver, Esquire, failed to properly represent him before the Immigration Court and this Board, and therefore, inter alia, prejudiced his ability to apply for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Act. The basis of the respondent’s argument rest on two claims; both that his prior counsel did not pursue post-conviction relief in federal court, and that they failed to properly investigate his immigration status in the United States.
 
In support of his motion, the respondent has submitted documentation indicating that Mr. Salazar was disbarred in 2010. The respondent has also submitted an affidavit and a complaint against Mr. Salazar to the Washington State Bar, as well as photocopies of receipts indicating various money exchanges between Mr. Salazar and the respondent. Through current counsel, the respondent has submitted evidence that he filed a Motion to Vacate Judgment on October 10, 2010, in the Douglas County Superior Court, State of California, pertaining to his July 26, 2001, conviction for conspiracy for unlawful delivery of methamphetamine.
 
Among other arguments, the respondent argues that even though he hired Mr. Salazar, it was Mr. Oliver who represented him at the hearing. Without more, we can not find that this fact alone constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent did not object to Mr. Oliver’s representation at the hearing (Tr. at 1), and the respondent has failed to demonstrate any contract indicating the agreement he entered into with either Mr. Salazar or Mr. Oliver. Moreover, the respondent has not filed a complaint with Mr. Oliver or otherwise contacted Mr. Oliver regarding the parameters of his representation or to alert him to the current complaint against Mr. Salazar. Accordingly, we do not find that the respondent has set forth sufficient evidence, in accordance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to adequately craft an ineffective assistance of counsel claim regarding his prior representation by Mr. Oliver, Esquire.
 
*2 Moreover, a respondent claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must show prejudice resulting from his attorney’s failure. Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1189 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Even assuming some link between Mr. Oliver and Mr. Salazar, the respondent fails to make this showing. Inasmuch as the basis of his current motion rests on the alleged prejudice that he suffered due to his prior counsel’s failure to file a motion for post-conviction relief of his criminal conviction, he has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge would have continued his case if such a motion had been filed prior to his hearing, or that the outcome might have changed were such a motion filed by Mr. Salazar or Mr. Oliver. The respondent’s counsel requested a continuance at the hearing so that he could file a motion to vacate the respondent’s criminal conviction “within two weeks” (Tr. at 5), but the Immigration Judge found no good cause for such a continuance because the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal would be denied due to the respondent’s inability to demonstrate that he was a lawful permanent resident (Tr. at 5).
 
The respondent argues, however, that his prior counsel failed to properly pursue relief from the legacy Immigration and Naturalization Service’s June 11, 2001, termination of his conditional residence status and the denial of his Form I-751 waiver. He argues that if prior counsel had so pursued such relief, he might have been able to demonstrate lawful permanent resident status for purposes of cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. We find this argument, at best, speculative. The respondent has presented no evidence in the current motion, or in prior proceedings, in support of his eligibility for lawful permanent resident status, and he has not indicated how he would pursue an appeal from his 2001 Form I-751 denial. Ineffective assistance of counsel is found only where the proceedings are “so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonably presenting his case.” Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993, 1001 (9th Cir 2005); Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent has not made such a showing; accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2011 WL 1373408 (BIA)
End of Document

2011 WL 1373403 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUBAIR SAEED AHMED A.K.A. ZUBAIR SAEED AHMED
File: AXXX XX6 211 - New York, NY
March 28, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Khagendra Gharti Chhetry, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maria Lurye
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on March 11, 2002, at which time we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to file a brief. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen proceedings on August 19, 2010. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent requests equitable tolling of these regulatory restrictions, and argues that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel from his prior counsel who represented him before the Board. He states that in 2002, he paid Mr. Charles A. Grutman, Esquire, $620 to file an appeal, but that the accompanying brief was not filed in a timely manner. The respondent argues that, subsequently, Mr. Grutman did not pursue any appeals or motions from this decision, but instead advised him to file an adjustment of status application under the Life Act. The respondent argues that if Mr. Grutman had filed a proper brief, he could have, inter alia, preserved the opportunity to file an appeal with the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, or should have, at least, filed a timely motion to reopen before the Board in 2002.
 
The Second Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that “¿iºn a situation where ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from having the opportunity to present his case for relief, the filing deadline for motions to reopen will be equitably tolled until the ineffective assistance is, or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.” See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 171 (2d Cir. 2006); citing Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000); see also Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 157 (2d Cir. 2006). In this case, the respondent has submitted two affidavits. In his most recent affidavit, executed on October 27, 2010, he avers that in March of 2002, he could not find Mr. Grutman, and was informed by his replacement, Mr. Robert Kuhnreich, that Mr. Grutman had stopped practicing law. He states that Mr. Kuhnreich, Esquire informed him that he should “not worry about his removal order” and should instead continue to pursue his Life Act application. The respondent states that he expressed “displeasure” with Mr. Grutman’s representation, and that he ultimately hired Mr. Ronald Gill, Esquire to continue with his adjustment application. He indicates that his adjustment application was denied on June 13, 2007, and that he subsequently hired Mr. John Finnell, Esquire to counsel him. He asserts that he continued to complain about his prior attorneys, but that Mr. Finnell, Esquire told him that he did not have to “worry about the removal case.”
 
*2 Along with his affidavit, the respondent has, inter alia, submitted evidence that he filed a complaint against Mr. Grutman, Esquire, with the Supreme Court Appellate Division, First Judicial Department, Departmental Disciplinary Committee, New York. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), see also Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 153, 157 fn.3 (2d Cir. 2006). The respondent has also submitted evidence that he has notified Mr. Grutman, Esquire, about his complaint.
 
According to the respondent’s affidavits, he indicated that he was dissatisfied with Mr. Grutman during discussions with Mr. Kuhnreich, Esquire in 2002. He also indicates, in his most recent affidavit, that he was aware of the Board’s dismissal of his appeal in 2002.¿FN1º However, the pending motion was not filed until August 19, 2010. See Cekic v. INS, supra, at 170 (“no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled”). While the respondent intimates that all of his subsequent attorneys failed to properly address his outstanding removal order, the respondent has not adequately explained why he did not file this motion until the receipt of his bag and baggage letter on July 26, 2010.¿FN2º This is so especially because, as the DHS argues in its response, the respondent’s appeal from the denial of his second adjustment of status application under the Life Act was apparently denied in 2009. Even were we to extend equitable tolling to the completion of this appeal process, the respondent still waited an additional year before filing the instant motion. In short, we find that the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence in presenting his arguments regarding Mr. Grutman’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008).
 
The respondent has also submitted evidence in support of his applications for adjustment of status, cancellation of removal, and asylum and withholding of removal. Regarding the respondent’s adjustment application, he has now filed evidence that his United States citizen sister filed a Form I-130 on his behalf. However, the respondent has not submitted evidence that he is currently eligible for adjustment of status, or that he would be excepted from the regulatory time requirements for such a motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Regarding the respondent’s cancellation of removal application, the DHS correctly argues that the respondent does not have a qualifying relative to support his application pursuant to section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. We also, similarly, find that the respondent has not indicated why he did not file this application at any time prior to his current motion.
 
*3 Regarding the respondent’s submitted Form I-589, we note that when the respondent conducted his airport interview in 1998, he indicated that he entered the United States “to work” and “for family” (Exh. 2). He also indicated that he had been residing in the United States since 1981 and that he had never been arrested anywhere in the world (Exh. 2). These statements contradict the claim submitted by the respondent in his recent Form I-589, and he has not explained why he failed to file an asylum application subsequent to his “flight from Pakistan” in 1981, or at any time prior to his most recent motion. The time and numerical limitation for motions to reopen do not apply to a motion to reopen proceedings to apply or reapply for asylum, based on changed country conditions, if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii); Malty v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 942, 945 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The motion must state the new facts to be proved and must be supported by evidentiary material. Matter of J-J-, supra. The alien must show a “reasonable likelihood of success on the merits so as to make it worthwhile to develop the issues further at a full evidentiary hearing.” Matter of A-N- & R-M-N-, 22 I&N Dec. 953 (BIA 1999). In short, we do not find that the respondent has presented new and material evidence which would support an exception to the timeliness requirements.
 
The pending motion will be, therefore, denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In his initial affidavit, dated August 16, 2010, he states that “Mr. Grutman, Esquire, did not inform me what happened to my appeal.” However, in his response to the DHS’ opposition, the respondent filed his second October 27, 2010, affidavit, indicating that he and Mr. Grutman, Esquire discussed the Board’s 2002 dismissal. Specifically, the respondent states that the “Immigration Judge ordered removal and Mr. Grutman wrote me a letter asking to remit $620 for appeal processing fee but failed to file a timely brief in my case … BIA rejected the appeal at that time and Mr. Grutman did not advise me to file a motion to reopen the case. Instead he advised me to submit an application for adjustment of status under the Life Act.” Accordingly, it appears that Mr. Grutman did, in fact, inform the respondent of the Board’s 2002 order.

2

In so finding, we note that the respondent’s initial August 16, 2010, affidavit (and the respondent’s affidavit to the Disciplinary Board) indicate that he was “shocked” when he received his bag and baggage letter. However, the respondent has failed to explain why he was so shocked. The respondent states, in his August 16, 2010, affidavit, that he consulted his current attorney on July 30, 2010, and was told that he received a bag and baggage letter because Mr. Grutman, Esquire, “did not file to the BIA a brief on time as required on my removal case.” However, the evidence in support of his motion indicates that a reasonable person would have known that he was under an order of removal. See Cekic v. INS, supra, at 170.

2011 WL 1373719 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RABIH ALMASRI
File: AXXX XX5 097 - Detroit, MI
March 25, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert M. Birach, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Theresa Bross
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On September 17, 2010, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’ September 1, 2009, decision denying his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). On December 13, 2010, the respondent filed this timely motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The Board will grant the motion and remand the record to the Immigration Judge.
 
The Immigration Judge noted the respondent’s “considerable equities,” but also considered the respondent’s “significant adverse factors,” including his criminal history, and determined that the respondent did not merit the desired relief as a matter of discretion. See Immigration Judge’s Decision at 8-10. In reaching his decision, the Immigration Judge emphasized that the respondent offered minimal evidence to corroborate key elements of his claims relating to his residency, hardship, assets, and rehabilitation.
 
The respondent seeks reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney. He specifically argues that his former attorney did not inform him that he needed to prove that he merited the relief as a matter of discretion, and that she failed to inform him of the type of evidence required to prove that he is deserving of a favorable exercise of discretion. With this motion, the respondent has offered evidence of his compliance with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Sswajje v. Ashcroft, 350 F.3d 528, 533 (6th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent has also offered evidence that he earned income in 2009, and that he filed income tax returns in 2007 and 2008. Further, he has offered a letter from Yemen Arab American Center for Economic and Social Service attesting to community service, and a letter from the mother of this United States citizen children describing his character.
 
The letter from the mother of the respondent’s children reveals that the respondent became the parent of his third United States citizen child after the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Exhibit 6. In light of this new evidence, the additional evidence pertaining to the respondent’s character, and the respondent’s “considerable equities” as noted by the Immigration Judge, we will grant this timely motion to reopen. Accordingly the motion to reopen will be granted, and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s equities in conjunction with his application for relief.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2011 WL 1373719 (BIA)
End of Document

2011 WL 1373694 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LILIA JUAREZ GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX6 603 - Bloomington, MN
March 23, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brianna Zuber Green, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel J. Pornschloegl
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On December 17, 2009, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States in absentia because she failed to appear for her hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on April 16, 2010, and that motion was denied on April 29, 2010.¿FN1º The respondent filed a timely appeal from this decision. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). Upon de novo review, we find that the respondent has not established that proceedings should be reopened.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he or she did not receive proper notice of the time and place of the hearing, or because he or she was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his or her own. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
In her motion, the respondent maintains that she was not served with notice in the Spanish language. However, the charging document is not required to be provided to an alien with a translation in her native language. See section 239 of the Act. As the record reflects and as noted by the Immigration Judge, the Notice to Appear (NTA) was received by the respondent by certified mail as shown by her signature on the receipt. She admits receiving the notice of hearing (NOH). Given this record, we find that the respondent was properly served with notice of her hearing.
 
The respondent maintains that she was told by someone named Virgilio not to attend her hearing. She argues in her motion to reopen that the reason she did not appear for her hearing is because she relied upon the advice of Virgilio, whom she thought had legal experience.
 
*2 Ineffective assistance of counsel may be an exceptional circumstance warranting rescission of an in absentia removal order. Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858, 862 (8th Cir. 2006). An alien asserting that ineffective assistance amounted to an exceptional circumstance must however fulfill the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d sub nom. Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Habchy, supra, at 862; see also Ochoa v. Holder, 604 F.3d 546, 548 n. 1 (8th Cir. 2010) (Lozada is “our substantive and procedural compass” in assessing ineffective assistance claims in immigration proceedings). The alien must (a) “support his claim with an affidavit alleging facts relevant to the ineffective assistance¿,º” (b) “inform the former counsel of the allegation and give her an opportunity to respond¿,º” and (c) show that he has either filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority regarding representation (i.e. the state bar association) or explain why he has not done so. Habchy, supra, at 862 (citing Lozada, 19 I. & N. at 639).
 
In certain circumstances, ineffective assistance of a non-attorney immigration consultant or notary can form the basis of relief in the context of removal proceedings. See, generally Albillo-De Leon v. Gonzales, 410 F.3d 1090, 1099 (9th Cir. 2005). However, it must be established that an alien suffered prejudice from the negligent advice of a non-attorney purporting to be a licensed attorney. See generally Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000) (an alien may be denied due process if he or she receives negligent advice from an individual who falsely purports to be an attorney); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). The alien must, however, establish that she suffered prejudice from the conduct of a non-attorney, she was unaware of the fraud, and she remained ignorant of it without any fault or want of diligence or care on her part. See Lopez v. INS, supra.
 
Here, although the respondent states that she believed Virgilio had legal experience (Respondent’s Affidavit in support of the Motion to Reopen), there is no evidence that he held himself out as a licensed attorney. Regardless, even if the respondent believed that Vergilio was an attorney or held himself out as such, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not satisfy the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra. See generally Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224, 1225-26 (11th Cir. 2008) (suggesting that Lozada applies to ineffective assistance of counsel claims rendered by non-attorneys); Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647, 651 (2d Cir. 2008) (finding that the Board’s decision to deny the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim against a non-attorney immigration consultant based on non-compliance with Lozada was “neither arbitrary nor capricious”).
 
*3 Under these circumstances, we do not find that the respondent received what may be tantamount to ineffective assistance to counsel. See generally Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that there is a “crucial difference” between ineffective assistance of counsel and ineffective assistance of an immigration consultant); see also Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943, 946 (9th Cir. 1999) (concluding that the erroneous advice of an immigration consultant does not constitute an exceptional circumstance requiring reopening of the proceedings). The respondent avoided her hearing based on a non-attorney’s advice despite being notified to appear and of the consequences for failing to do so.
 
We note that the respondent has proffered a copy of Virgilio’s business card on appeal. We do not consider evidence first submitted on appeal; rather, we review the record before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 & n. 10 (BIA 1984); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988). Even if we were to construe the respondent’s additional evidence as a motion to remand, the respondent has not shown that this evidence is new and previously unavailable. Moreover, the card does not establish that Virgilio held himself out as an attorney. We therefore find that a remand is not warranted.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

The decision was mailed on May 5, 2010.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MATEUS FREITAS-MORAES
File: AXXX XX3 643 - San Antonio, TX
March 23, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Julie A. Endy, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nancy K. Vande Walle
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 28, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on October 9, 2002. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i),(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent reiterates that he did not receive proper notice of his hearing. We agree with the decision of the Immigration Judge.¿FN1º The record reflects that the respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (NTA) on April 27, 2002. See I.J. at 1; Exh. 1. Following various continuances, the San Antonio Immigration Court sent a hearing notice to the respondent’s former attorney of record, Jose Salvador Tellez, Esquire, scheduling the respondent’s hearing for October 9, 2002. See I.J. at 2; Exh. 2. There is no indication that the hearing notice was returned to the Immigration Court and the respondent acknowledges that his former attorney received the notice.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of his hearing was provided to his attorney of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (finding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). The record contains a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative (Form EOIR-28) for Mr. Tellez, as required by regulation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.4(a).
 
We are also not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that he did not hire Mr. Tellez and that after the respondent was released from custody he did not have any contact with Mr. Tellez or his associates. Here, the respondent does not claim that he did not know Mr. Tellez, he acknowledges that he was aware of Mr. Tellez’s efforts in securing his release from custody, and form EOIR-28 was properly filed in the respondent’s case. We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent received proper notice.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent argues exceptional circumstances because his attorney did not inform him about the date and time of his hearing, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is untimely as it was not filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see also Vaz Dos Reis v. Holder, 606 F.3d 1, 5 (1st Cir. 2010) (stating that with respect to motions to reopen in absentia proceedings courts typically treat ineffective assistance of counsel claims as an exceptional circumstance). In addition, we agree with the determination of the Immigration Judge that even if equitable tolling is taken into consideration, the respondent has failed to establish due diligence in filing his motion to reopen. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 220 (5th Cir. 2008) (finding that a request for equitable tolling was essentially a contention that the Board should exercise sua sponte authority over an untimely motion, and that the court lacks jurisdiction to review a decision by the Board not to exercise this authority).
 
We also find that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. The respondent was personally served with a NTA on April 27, 2002, ordering him to appear before an Immigration Judge in San Antonio, Texas at a date and time to be set. See Exh. 1. He has not established what efforts he took to determine the status of his removal proceeding after receiving the NTA more than 7 years before he filed his motion to reopen. We find no basis for sua sponte reopening. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
We turn now to the respondent’s suggestion on appeal that he is eligible for adjustment of status based upon his marriage to a United States citizen. Attached to his brief on appeal he submitted documentation relating to a visa petition filed on his behalf. We do not consider evidence first submitted on appeal; rather, we review the record before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 & n.10 (BIA 1984); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988) Even if we were to construe the respondent’s additional evidence as a motion to remand, we would deny it because the respondent was personally served with a NTA which indicates that he received oral notice in the Spanish language of the consequences of failing to appear for his removal hearing. He is therefore statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status.¿FN2º See section 240(b)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(7); see also Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998) (explaining that an in absentia deportation order need not always be rescinded before it can be reopened). In sum, we find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

We observe that the Immigration Judge made a clearly erroneous factual determination. In particular, the Immigration Judge erroneously stated that a motion to continue a hearing scheduled for June 22, 2002, was granted by the Immigration Judge. See I.J. at 2. The record, however, reflects that the hearing was scheduled for June 20, 2002, and that the motion to continue filed by the respondent’s former attorney on May 28, 2002, was denied. A hand-written notation on the bottom of the motion to continue indicates that it was denied on June 13, 2002. Moreover, the record reflects that on June 20, 2002, the respondent appeared for a hearing with his former attorney and admitted to the charges listed in the Notice to Appear. See Exh. 1; Tr. at 1-2. We find, however, that the Immigration Judge’s factual determination to be harmless as he otherwise correctly determined that the respondent received proper notice. Neither party raises this issue on appeal.

Therefore he has not established prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. See Exh. 1; Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009) (discussing prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status).

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JACKSON NKESHA
File: AXXX XX2 322 - Boston, MA
March 22, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s November 18, 2010, decision denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Rwanda, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on April 27, 2004 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). On January 25, 2007, the respondent was convicted in the Waltham District Court, Massachusetts, for the offenses of witness intimidation and violating an abuse prevention order in violation of Massachusetts General Laws Chapter 268 section 13B(1) and Chapter 209(A) section 7, respectively (I.J. at 1). On June 23, 2008, the respondent was ordered removed from the United States by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 1). The respondent was thereafter removed from the United States in December 2008.
 
On October 22, 2010, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen based on the vacatur of his conviction for witness intimidation (I.J. at 2). The respondent argued that his conviction for violating an abuse prevention order is not a crime involving moral turpitude and that the motion deadline should be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his criminal proceedings (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge denied the motion for lack of jurisdiction and, alternatively, as time-barred (I.J. at 2-3). The respondent appeals.
 
Despite the respondent’s contentions on appeal, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion for lack of jurisdiction (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3) (2010). The United States Court of Appeals for the First Circuit, where this case arises, has held that the amendment to the regulation precluding consideration of a motion to reopen after an alien has departed the country did not abrogate the Attorney General’s authority to enforce the regulation barring, after the alien’s departure from United States, a motion to reopen by or on behalf of the alien who was subject of removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Pena-Muriel v. Gonzales, 489 F. 3d 438 (1st Cir. 2007); see also Matter of Armendariz-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646, 660 (BIA 2008).
 
Further, that a vacatur of conviction upon which an alien’s deportability was premised may be an appropriate basis for reopening a deportation order does not establish a due process right to such reopening after the alien has departed the country. See Pena-Muriel v. Gonzales, supra. An alien has received due process where, at the time of his departure, he had been convicted of crimes triggering the relevant deportation provisions, his removal proceeding came to a lawful conclusion before his conviction was vacated, and, with benefit of appropriate notice, he received a hearing before an Immigration Judge and an opportunity for appeal. See id. (due process does not require giving alien continuous opportunities to attack executed removal orders years beyond alien’s departure from the country). Thus, in this case, the Immigration Judge properly concluded that he lacked jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion to reopen filed after his removal from the United States.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ANNA MARGEBADZE

March 21, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tatiana S. Aristova, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa A. Compagno
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The Department of Homeland Security filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s July 8, 2010, decision, which granted the respondent’s motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings on the issue of her inadmissibility under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i). The respondent opposes the DHS’s motion to reconsider and has submitted a separate motion to remand and stay removal.
 
The DHS argues that the Board issued its July 8, 2010, decision granting the respondent’s motion without considering the arguments stated in its opposition brief. We first point out that there was no DHS opposition to the motion associated with the record of proceedings at the time of our last decision. The record does include the DHS’s request for an extension of time to respond to the respondent’s motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, but we apparently did not rule on the request. In any event, we have reviewed the DHS’s untimely opposition brief, a copy of which was submitted with its motion to reconsider, and conclude that it would not have altered our decision to grant the respondent’s ineffective assistance motion.
 
We reject the DHS’s argument that the respondent did not suffer prejudice by her former counsel’s concession to the fraud charge. The respondent acknowledges that arriving aliens, with a limited exception that is not implicated in this case, may not have an adjustment of status application adjudicated in removal proceedings. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009). However, the finding of inadmissibility in the removal proceedings based on the fraud charge impedes the respondent’s ability to adjust her status in the proper jurisdiction, United States Customs and Immigration Enforcement, unless she is able to qualify for a waiver. Thus, her counsel’s concession to the charge caused her prejudice. Accordingly, we deny the DHS’s motion and again remand the record to the Immigration Judge pursuant to our July 8, 2010, decision.
 
Our decision denying the DHS’s motion renders moot the respondent’s separate motion to remand and stay her removal.
 
ORDER: The DHS’s motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings that are not inconsistent with our July 8, 2010, decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Motion to remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s October 13, 2010, decision denying his motion for a continuance to seek post-conviction relief.¿FN1º During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand. The motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on February 5, 2002 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). On March 3, 2010, the respondent was convicted for the offense of possession of a stolen vehicle in violation of Nevada Revised Statutes section 205.273. On September 14, 2010, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged based on his conviction (I.J. at 3-4). On October 13, 2010, the Immigration Judge held an additional hearing and denied the respondent’s request for a continuance to seek post-conviction relief. The respondent appealed the denial of his request for a continuance.¿FN2º
 
During the pendency of the respondent’s appeal, he filed a motion to remand including evidence that his March 3, 2010, conviction has been set aside by the criminal court at least in part on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to this filing. Under these circumstances, we find it necessary to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to determine whether the respondent’s conviction has been vacated with the burden of proof on the DHS to show that the conviction was not set aside solely to avoid immigration consequences. See Nath v. Gonzales, 467 F.3d 1185 (9th Cir. 2006).
 
Accordingly, the motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley

IN RE: LEONEL PINILLA PEREZ A.K.A. LEONEL PINILLA A.K.A. LIONER PINILLA A.K.A. LEONEL PINELLE A.K.A. LIONEL PANAMA A.K.A. LIONEL PINILLA A.K.A. LEONEL PINELLIA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On January 24, 2011, the respondent submitted an untimely motion to reopen his removal proceedings that administratively final on September 20, 2010, when the Board dismissed his appeal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion is denied.
 
The respondent asserts that he is now eligible to pursue either a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act or cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act because a state court granted a motion he filed pursuant to New York State Criminal Procedure Section 440.10(I)(h), and vacated the judgment of his conviction. A copy of the court’s order is appended to the respondent’s motion. However, the court order does not reflect that the criminal conviction at issue was vacated. Rather, court found that a further hearing “on the issue of whether the ineffective assistance of counsel caused any prejudice” to the respondent was required (Court Order at 7). Thus, the respondent’s conviction remains effective for immigration purposes, and sua sponte reopening is not warranted based on the evidence now before us. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes

2011 WL 891905 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDWARD MERCADO A.K.A. EDUARDO MERCADO A.K.A. EDUARDO MERCADO BARRAZA A.K.A. EDDIE RICARDO
File: AXXX XX3 759 - Los Angeles, CA
March 1, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David W. Williams, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, moves to reopen these proceedings seeking a further opportunity apply for adjustment of status. Section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.§ 1255(a). Motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of our final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Our final order was issued on May 27, 2009. Therefore, the instant motion, filed on July 6, 2010, is untimely. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response, so it will be deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The motion will be granted.
 
In our last decision, we concluded that the respondent’s 2002 conviction for possession of a controlled substance, which resulted in deferred adjudication, remains a conviction for immigration purposes under the law in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. We agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s first conviction in 1990 for possession of a controlled substance was accorded deferred adjudication, and would have qualified for Federal First Offender Act (FFOA) treatment. 18 U.S.C. § 3607; Matter of Manrique, 21 I&N Dec. 58 (BIA 1995) (federal policy of leniency to first-time drug offenders would be extended to those aliens who had not previously been accorded first-offender treatment under any law); see also Lujan-Armendariz v. INS, 222 F.3d 728 (9th Cir. 2000). The second conviction in 2002, however, would not have been afforded FFOA treatment under federal law, and we agreed that it remained a conviction under immigration law regardless of the respondent’s satisfactory completion of the diversionary program.
 
The respondent argues in his motion that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during proceedings and while the matter was on appeal. However, we need not reach this issue. Since our last decision, the Ninth Circuit has distinguished a suspended sentence, which is a predicate for a conviction under section 101 (a)(48) of the Act, from “an unconditional non-incarceratory suspended sanction,” concluding that the latter cannot be a predicate for a conviction for purposes of removal proceedings. Retuta v. Holder, 591 F.3d 1181, 1188 (9th Cir. 2010). In the respondent’s criminal proceeding for the second offense, he pled guilty, and the judge found that there was a factual basis for the defendant’s plea. However, the judge deferred entry of judgment and imposed a $100 diversion restitution fee and noted a 1 day sentence of time served.
 
*2 The respondent was placed on 24 months deferred entry of judgment, which he completed satisfactorily. He was then allowed to withdraw the plea of guilty, which was set aside and the case was dismissed pursuant to section 100.3 of the California Penal Code. Given the intervening case law, and considering the totality of the circumstances, we find that the unopposed motion presents exceptional circumstances warranting sua sponte reopening pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). On remand, the parties should address the applicability of Retuta v. Holder, supra, to this case. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted sua sponte.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2011 WL 891905 (BIA)

2011 WL 891878 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUANA ALVARADO JUANA GONZALEZ A.K.A. JUANA POLANCO
Files: AXXX XX8 839 - New York, NY
AXXX XX3 472
February 24, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Andrew L. Friedman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of the Dominican Republic, are mother and daughter. On May 20, 2002, the Board summarily dismissed the respondents’ appeal for failure to file a promised brief or statement. Subsequently, we located the respondents’ brief which had been filed at the Board on September 8, 1999. On August 5, 2002, we vacated the May 20, 2002, decision as to each respondent. On August 22, 2002, the Board affirmed without opinion the results of the decision below and designated the Immigration Judge’s decision as the final agency determination. In a decision on September 3, 2009, we denied as untimely a motion to reopen proceedings filed only by the respondent daughter and declined to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings.¿FN1º A motion to reopen proceedings, filed on behalf of both respondents, was received by the Board on June 16, 2010. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondents claim that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance by Vina Fleisig, Esquire, their former attorney. This motion to reopen is untimely as to both respondents (filed more than 90 days after our final administrative decision on August 22, 2002) and number-barred as to the respondent daughter (exceeds the single motion to reopen permitted). See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). However, the respondents seek reopening based on equitable tolling of the time period for reopening based on the ineffective assistance claim.
 
On January 7, 2009, the United States Attorney General issued a decision setting forth standards for claims involving ineffective assistance of counsel, but this decision was subsequently vacated by the current Attorney General. Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). Pending rulemaking procedures, the Board was directed to “apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed.”
 
Aliens raising an ineffective assistance claim in a motion can have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129-133 (2d Cir. 2000). Aliens seeking to prevail on an ineffective assistance claim must establish 1) that counsel’s performance was so ineffective as to have impinged on the fundamental fairness of the proceedings because counsel’s conduct was such that competent counsel would have acted otherwise; and 2) counsel’s performance must have resulted in prejudice to the aliens. See Romero v. United States INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005), citing U.S. v. Perez, 330 F.3d 97, 101 (2d Cir. 2001). Under both Compean and pre-Compean standards, due diligence in pursuit of all claims has been required. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir.2006) (“¿Nºo matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.”).
 
*2 We acknowledge that the lead respondent/mother has complied with the complaint requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Zheng v. U.S. Department of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-47 (2d Cir. 2005). However, compliance with the Lozada requirements does not, in and of itself, warrant equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance. In the documents submitted in support of the motion, e.g. Exhibit D at 29 and Exhibit Q at 73, the respondents have not indicated when they retained current counsel and/or became aware of any claimed ineffective assistance. We find that the respondents have not affirmatively demonstrated that they exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled. See Cekic v. INS, supra; Iavorski v. United States INS, supra. Separately, we note that the respondents’ ineffective assistance claim is primarily based on failure of prior counsel to file any brief on appeal (Exh. D at 28; Exh. Q at 73). The Board previously acknowledged that a brief on appeal was submitted and vacated the May 20, 2002, decision erroneously dismissing the appeal for failure to file a promised brief or statement. Consequently, the respondents’ contentions that their prior attorney failed to file a brief on appeal are without factual foundation. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and request for a stay of removal are denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the procedural history recited in the respondents’ pending motion is inconsistent with the above information obtained from the record of proceedings.

2011 WL 891876 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BETH CARDONAS A.K.A. BEATRIZ AURORA CARDONAS-LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX5 159 - Los Angeles, CA
February 24, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos Martinez-Couoh, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay
 
*1 On August 19, 1998, the respondent failed to appear for her removal hearing and was ordered removed in absentia by the Immigration Judge. The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied in a March 1, 1999, decision. The respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial, but the Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal in a March 22, 2002, decision. On September 1, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board based on the alleged ineffectiveness of her former counsel. For the reasons set out below, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on March 22, 2002. Generally, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). A request for rescission of an in absentia order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date that the order of removal is issued. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) and 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The present motion was filed on September 1, 2010, well beyond the filing deadlines. In addition, the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen that was denied by the Immigration Judge on March 1, 1999.
 
However, aliens raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in a motion can have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Aliens relying on equitable tolling have the burden of demonstrating that they exercised due diligence in discovering the fraud or error.
 
We find that the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen does not qualify for equitable tolling. The respondent has not demonstrated that she exercised the requisite diligence in filing the instant motion. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on August 19, 1998, and on March 22, 2002, the Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s March 1, 1999, decision denying her motion to reopen. However, the respondent waited until September 1, 2010, to file the instant motion, and has not demonstrated what she did between March 2002 and September 2010 to protect her rights or when she definitely learned of her prior counsel’s alleged incompetence (see id). Given the respondent’s delay in obtaining her current counsel, and the lack of evidence regarding steps she took to preserve his rights from 2002 to 2010, we find that the respondent did not act with due diligence. See Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096 (9th Cir. 2007) (refusing to toll where immigrant failed to investigate suspicions of incompetence for 6 months). In any event, the respondent has not submitted the requisite application for cancellation of removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for a stay is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2011 WL 891876 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 On October 5, 2010, the Board denied a motion filed by the respondent seeking to reopen the Board’s February 28, 2001, decision. The respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider that decision on November 1, 2010. On January 10, 2011, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit issued an order incorporating a stipulation filed by the parties. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
The stipulation filed by the parties remanded the record to allow the Board to consider whether the respondent established that he acted with due diligence in pursuing a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). In view of the court’s remand order, and upon further review of the record, including the indications of ineffective assistance of counsel and the representations made by the respondent regarding due diligence, we will reopen this case. On remand, the respondent should be afforded an opportunity to pursue a section 212(c) waiver of inadmissibility. We make no findings with regard to the final determination as to the respondent’s eligibility for the requested waiver.
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be entered. In view of our decision reopening this case, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is moot.
 
ORDER: The decision of the Board dated October 5, 2010, is vacated, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

IN RE: LUIS ARMANDO LAINEZ-CERNA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Shaheewa Jarrett Gelin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of Honduras. On November 23, 2010, an Immigration Judge ordered his removal from the United States noting that the respondent did not file for any form of relief from removal. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent states in his Notice of Appeal that he is married to a United States citizen and is the father of citizen children. He requests an opportunity to remain in the United States. However, the respondent has not presented any evidence indicating that a visa petition has been filed on his behalf. We further are unable to identify any form of relief from removal that the respondent is eligible to seek. The respondent suggests on appeal that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. He has not satisfied any of the procedural requirements for establish that claim. See Dakane v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 342 F.3d 1219 (11th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Carol King

2011 WL 891899 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ORELLANA-GUTIERREZ
File: AXXX XX7 817 - New York, NY
February 22, 2011
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hedwin Salmen-Navarro, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF THE DHS:
 
Timothy Maguire
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 3, 2010, when the Board denied the respondent’s third motion to reopen as untimely and number-barred and not excepted from the filing requirements imposed on motions to reopen removal proceedings. The Board rejected the respondent’s assertion that the time and numerical limits for filing his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled due to the alleged deficient performance of two non attorneys, both of whom allegedly advised and or represented the respondent after the completion of his proceedings before both the Immigration Judge and the Board. The Board concluded that the respondent did not substantially comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), did not demonstrate the required prejudice, and did not show due diligence. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra; Wang v. Bd. of Immigration Appeals, 508 F.3d 710 (2d Cir. 2007); Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d, 481, 486 (2d Cir. 2010). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). On December 10, 2010, the respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, filed a fourth motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
To the extent the respondent seeks reconsideration, the motion is untimely. See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Moreover, to the extent the respondent’s motion rests on previously raised issues related to the alleged deficient performance of one or both of the non attorneys, the respondent has not identified any issue that was not considered in the Board’s previous decision, nor has he identified any error of fact or law in that decision that would alter the outcome. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). The respondent’s apparent request for reconsideration is therefore denied.
 
*2 Similarly, the respondent’s present motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred and has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the time and numerical limitations imposed on motions to reopen removal proceedings. As previously found, the respondent has not demonstrated the required prejudice stemming from his prior attorney’s alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra; Debeatham v. Holder, supra. See also Matter of Compean, supra. The respondent has not sufficiently described or submitted or pointed to any evidence supporting his claim that his two United States citizen step children, who were not included in the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal, would suffer the requisite level of hardship upon the respondent’s removal. Nor does the record support the respondent’s claim that he was at any stage in his proceedings before either the Immigration Judge, the Board, or the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, either unaware that he was granted the privilege of voluntary departure or not properly advised of his obligations in connection with such grant. Further, the respondent has not shown due diligence in making his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Wang v. Bd. of Immigration Appeals, supra.
 
Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated an “exceptional situation” that would warrant sua sponte reopening of these proceedings as a matter of discretion. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating “the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999).
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided us with an adequate reason to overlook the fact that his motion is untimely and number-barred. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion and related request for a stay of removal are denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

MOTION

Daniel J. Redwood
