2010 WL 5559161 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KATALIN EVA HORVATH
File: AXXX XX6 331 - San Francisco, CA
December 27, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J. Mark Kang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGES:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Hungary, adjusted her status to lawful permanent resident on April 29, 1991, retroactive to April 11, 1989, the date on which she was granted conditional status. On June 28, 1996, the respondent was convicted of commercial burglary and grand theft in violation of sections 459, 460(b), and 487(a) of the California Penal Code (Exh. 1, 1A). A Notice to Appear was issued on December 11, 1997, but was not filed until April 1, 2003. On June 10, 2004, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged, and ordered her removed to Hungary. The respondent appealed the removal order but then withdrew her appeal (BIA, dated September 28, 2004, at 1).
 
On August 5, 2010, the respondent filed a motion styled “Respondent Request to Review Her Removal Proceedings and Her Appeal.” The respondent argued that the removal order was incorrect because at the time she was convicted, the offenses of burglary and grand theft were not aggravated felonies. The Immigration Judge construed the motion as a motion to reopen, and on September 3, 2010, denied the motion as untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2010). The respondent now appeals. The respondent’s request for a waiver of the appellate filing fee is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that her counsel during the removal proceedings was ineffective (DHS Br. filed September 2, 2010, at 2; Form EOIR-26, attached statement at 1). As the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) points out the respondent did not raise this argument in her motion before the Immigration Judge (DHS Br. at 2). She cannot argue for the first time on appeal that her counsel during the 2004 removal proceedings was ineffective (DHS Br. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(b) (providing that the statement of the basis of appeal must “specifically identify the findings of fact, the conclusions of law, or both, that are being challenged”).
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent has not presented evidence of her compliance with any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998) (DHS Br. at 2-3, 4). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). In Matter of Lozada, supra, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires that (1) the motion be supported by an affidavit of the applicant setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and her legal representative and what actions were to be taken on her behalf; (2) the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) the motion reflect whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. The record does not contain any of the required documentation.
 
Furthermore, the allegations made regarding ineffective assistance of counsel appear disingenuous. For example, the respondent alleges that her prior counsel did not inform her of, or bring her to, her final removal hearing (Form EOIR-26, attached statement at 1). The record indicates that the respondent was present on June 10, 2004, when the Immigration Judge ordered her removed to Hungary (Form EOIR-26, attached statement at 1; Tr. at 7-22).¿FN1º
 
The respondent also suggests that prior counsel did not inform her that she was eligible for relief from removal under INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001) (Form EOIR-26, attached statement at 1). The argument is not persuasive. The transcript reveals that at the June 10, 2004, hearing, prior counsel raised the issue of whether the respondent was eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) pursuant to INS v. St. Cyr, supra (Tr. at 18).
 
More importantly, INS v. St. Cyr, supra, does not help the respondent (DHS Br. filed November 5, 2010, at 4-6; DHS Br. filed September 2, 2010, at 2-3). In INS v. St. Cyr, supra, the Supreme Court held that the restrictions on section 212(c) relief do not apply retroactively to an alien who pled guilty before April 24, 1996, the effective date of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (“AEDPA”). The respondent was convicted after a bench trial,¿FN2º and the convictions were sustained on June 28, 1996, 2 months after the AEDPA’s effective date (DHS Br. filed November 5, 2010, at 4-6; Respondent’s Br. filed September 20, 2010, at 4;Tr. at 12, 17; Exh. 1, 1A). See INS v. Velasco-Medina, 305 F.3d 839, 849-850 (9th Cir. 2002) (explaining that relief under former section 212(c) was unavailable for an alien who was convicted after the AEDPA’s date of enactment); Kelava v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 1120, 1124-25 (9th Cir. 2006); Armendariz-Montoya v. Sonchik, 291 F.3d 1116, 1121 (9th Cir. 2002). Therefore, because the respondent cannot rely on INS v. St Cyr for relief, she cannot persuasively claim ineffective assistance of counsel based on misapplication of the law (DHS Br. filed November 5, 2010, at 4-6; DHS Br. filed September 2, 2010, at 2-3).
 
*3 The respondent argued in her motion, and again on appeal, that she is eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), because her 1996 burglary and grand theft convictions predated the enactment of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996 (“IIRIRA”), and were not considered aggravated felonies at the time the convictions were entered (Respondent’s Br. filed September 20, 2010, at 2). She states that the offenses were not aggravated felonies because the term of imprisonment imposed was 4 years, and at the time, the definition of aggravated felony required the imposed term of imprisonment to be at least 5 years (Respondent’s Br. filed September 20, 2010, at 2; Respondent Request to Review Her Removal Proceedings and Her Appeal at 4).¿FN3º Congress, however, expressly stated that IIRIRA’s amendments to the definition of “aggravated felony” apply retroactively. See Pub. L. 104-208, § 321 (c), 110 Stat. 3009 (“The amendments made by this section shall apply to actions taken on or after the day of the enactment of this Act ¿September 30, 1996º, regardless of when the conviction occurred ….”). See also Alvarez-Barajas v. Gonzales, 418 F.3d 1050, 1054-55 (9th Cir. 2005); Matter of Yeung, 21 I&N Dec. 610, 614 (1997) (stating “¿aºny presumption against retroactivity cannot apply where Congress has clearly stated that a statute is to be applied retroactively.”). Having been convicted of an aggravated felony, the respondent is not eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). For this reason, she cannot claim that her prior counsel was ineffective in not pursuing relief under section 212(h).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent states that her final hearing was on October 12, 2004, but the record indicates that the hearing proceedings were closed on June 10, 2004 (Tr. at 22).

2

The respondent concedes that under Ninth Circuit precedent she is not eligible for relief under former section 212(c) because her 1996 convictions resulted from a trial (Respondent’s Br. filed September 20, 2010, at 4).

3

The respondent does not otherwise dispute that her burglary and grand theft convictions are aggravated felonies under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G).

IN RE: SHAWN R. HALL

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brian G. McDonnell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On November 10, 2010, the respondent submitted an untimely Notice of Appeal with respect to the Immigration Judge’s April 22, 2010, decision denying a motion to reopen. A Notice of Appeal was due to be filed no later than May 24, 2010. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b) (Notice of Appeal shall be filed within 30 days after the stating of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of an Immigration Judge’s written decision); Matter of Liadov, 23 I&N Dec. 990 (BIA 2006). As the respondent’s Notice of Appeal was not filed until months after that date, the appeal is untimely. Failure to file a timely appeal divests this Board of jurisdiction.
 
To the extent that the respondent now avers he was provided ineffective assistance of counsel by his criminal defense attorney who did not notify him of the collateral consequences of his criminal plea, this aspect of his submission is in the nature of a motion to reopen. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). However, it is untimely as it was filed more than a year after the Immigration Judge’s final administrative decision, and he has not provided any evidence substantiating this claim. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). To the extent that the respondent suggests that his criminal conviction is untenable in light of the holding in Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), we note that this decision does not change the finality of his conviction for immigration purposes, unless and until it is overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996). Therefore, reopening on this basis is not warranted. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
In sum, the Immigration Judge’s decision is final, and the records are returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 5559159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMED JAHANGIR
File: AXXX XX7 555 - Hartford, CT
December 27, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Meri S. Ponist, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lincoln Jalelian
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board issued an administratively final removal order in this case on June 15, 2006. The respondent’s three prior motions have been denied. On August 2, 2010, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen. The respondent reasserts his prior ineffective assistance claim against the counsel who represented him on appeal and against the counsel who represented him before the Immigration Judge.
 
We will not address the respondent’s claim that he received ineffective assistance from counsel Anthony D. Collins at his hearings before the Immigration Judge beyond commenting that there is no evidence in the record that Mr. Collins represented the respondent in these proceedings. In fact, contrary to the respondent’s declaration, the counsel who appeared on his behalf at his last immigration hearing was Alec J. Niziolek, Esquire, who is not mentioned at all in the motion (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 1, 5).¿FN1º The fact that Mr. Niziolek was affiliated with Mr. Collins’ law firm does not create an ineffective assistance claim against Mr. Collins absent specific evidence that Collins performed a direct role in the case that unfairly prejudiced its outcome. Individual attorneys, not law firms or other associations, must enter their appearances in cases before the Executive Office for Immigration Review and are individually accountable for their actions. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.17, 1003.101 et seq.
 
With respect to the respondent’s argument that he received ineffective assistance from his appellate counsel, M. Najmul Alam, warranting reopening of his proceedings or, alternatively, reissuance of the June 15, 2006, decision dismissing his appeal, we note that our last two decisions rejected his ineffective assistance claim against Mr. Alam for failure to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has belatedly complied with the procedural steps, including the requirement that he notify the accused counsel and provide him an opportunity to respond. However, Mr. Alam’s response forcefully denies the respondent’s claims and states that he zealously represented the respondent on his appeal, timely notified the respondent of the Board’s decision and his options to seek further administrative or judicial review. According to Mr. Alam, the respondent then informed him that he did not wish him to perform any further services in his immigration case (MTR, Tab F). We find Mr. Alam’s response more persuasive than the respondent’s declaration, particularly in light of the material inaccuracies in the same declaration regarding Mr. Collins’ role in his case.¿FN2º
 
*2 Accordingly, we are not persuaded that the respondent has demonstrated a basis to equitably toll the general restrictions on motions and deny the motion as time and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129-135 (2d Cir. 2000)(aliens relying on equitable tolling must establish that their constitutional right to due process was violated by counsel’s conduct and that they exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled). See also Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130-32 (2d Cir. 2008) (an alien is required to exercise due diligence both before and after he has or should have discovered ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s arguments that his friend, Rashid, ineffectively assisted him are too vague to constitute a colorable claim, particularly since it does not appear that he attempted to assist the respondent until more than a year after his appeal was dismissed and that his assistance was principally related to the visa petition proceedings.

IN RE: AVRIL WARD
File: AXXX XX1 874 - Miami, FL
December 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth Panzer, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent’s motion is untimely filed and number-barred and has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the filing requirements imposed on motions to reopen removal proceedings. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i), of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).¿FN1º Similarly, the respondent’s request for reopening to pursue her application for adjustment of status based on a pending Form I-360 petition as an abused spouse of a United States citizen, is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Act. That section contains a special rule for battered spouses which provides an exception to the motions’ deadlines. The applicable deadline can be waived where someone wishes to self-petition for adjustment of status as an abused spouse of a United States citizen, has provided a copy of the petition, and files the motion within one year of the entry of the final order of removal. This time limitation can be waived in the exercise of discretion if an alien can demonstrate extraordinary circumstances or extreme hardship to a child. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Act.
 
The record reflects the Board entered a final order of removal on April 6, 2004. However, the instant motion was not filed until June 1, 2010. In this instance, in order to apply the statutory exception found in section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Act to the filing deadline, the motion had to have been submitted within one year of the entry of the final order of removal. As noted above, the record reflects that more than 6 years have passed since the Board entered its final order of removal and the respondent filed the instant motion. Furthermore, in order to waive this time limitation, the respondent must demonstrate extraordinary circumstances or extreme hardship to her child. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv)(III) of the Act. Despite her pending petition, the respondent has presented no evidence as to any extraordinary circumstances or extreme hardship to a child, to cause us to exercise favorable discretion in this instance.
 
Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated that this case involves an exceptional situation for sua sponte reopening under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). On this record, the respondent has provided us with no reason to overlook the fact that her motion is untimely and number-barred. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion and related request for a stay of removal are denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

By motion filed on November 18, 2010, the respondent withdrew her asserted ineffective assistance of counsel claim. However, to the extent the respondent continues to claim ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not sufficiently shown patent error or prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).

IN RE: KARINA LISSETH GUEVARA-MARTINEZ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hilario Mercado, Jr., Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador and lawful permanent resident of the United States, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 16, 2010, which found that she was removable as charged. While the appeal was pending, the respondent filed an Emergency Motion to Terminate Removal Proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be granted and proceedings terminated.
 
The DHS served the respondent with a Notice to Appear that alleged that she had been convicted on April 2, 2009, in Virginia of misdemeanor embezzlement, for which she was sentenced to 12 months incarceration, 8 months suspended, and charged that she was removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i) (convicted of crime involving moral turpitude). On appeal, the respondent provided a November 23, 2010, order showing that the State court vacated the respondent’s April 2, 2009, conviction based on the finding that the respondent had established that her attorney’s failure to advise her of the immigration consequences of her guilty plea constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. We agree with the respondent that her conviction is no longer valid for immigration purposes. Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878, 879 (BIA 2006). There is no other factual allegation or charge of removability lodged against the respondent. Therefore, we find that the respondent is no longer removable from the United States. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is granted, the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 16, 2010, is vacated, and removal proceedings are terminated.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: LEONIDA MONTERON SARMIENTO A.K.A. LEONIDA MIRANDA MONTERON ROMEO LAGDAAN SARMIENTO
Files: AXXX XX7 848 - Chicago, IL
AXXX XX7 849
December 22, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Shannon Shepherd, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amy E. Greene
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondents, a married couple who are natives and citizens of the Philippines, move this Board to reconsider our June 29, 2010, final decision.¿FN1º 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). In that decision, we dismissed the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 30, 2009, decision denying adjustment of status. Section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent entered the United States as a non-immigrant visitor on July 3, 2003, with authority to remain until July 1, 2004. She filed a valid Form I-140 and an Application for Adjustment of Status (Form I-485) with the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Service (USCIS) on May 28, 2004. Ultimately, the application for adjustment was denied on October 20, 2005, because the respondent failed to provide the required certificate pursuant to section 212 (a)(5(C) of the Act (an alien seeking entry to perform labor as a health-care worker other than a physician must present a certificate from the Commission on Graduates of Foreign Nursing Schools or equivalent in order to be admissible). See also section 245(a) of the Act. The respondent filed a second Form I-485 with the USCIS on December 5, 2005. On October 17, 2007, this application was denied because the respondent had not maintained valid non-immigrant status since her last entry as required by section 245(c) of the Act, and she did not qualify for an exception because she had been out of status since July 1, 2004, well beyond the 180 day excepted period under section 245(k) of the Act. See also Matter of L-K-, 23 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2004).
 
A motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must include, in relevant part, the following: (1) an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority; and (2) if there has been a change in law, a reference to the relevant statute, regulation, or precedent and an explanation of how the outcome of the Board’s decision is materially affected by the change. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). This motion argues essentially that the respondents were in the United States within a period of authorized stay while the applications for adjustment of status were pending, and therefore the respondents had not been out of status for over 180 days when they filed their second application for adjustment of status.
 
*2 However, where a first application is denied and a second adjustment application is filed, the period after which the nonimmigrant status expired and during which the first adjustment of status application was pending counts against the 180-day period. See USCIS HQDOMO 70/23.1-P AD06-07 memorandum on Applicability of Section 245(k) to certain Employment-Based Adjustment of Status Application under section 245(a) of the Act. The period that the respondent was out of status began on July 1, 2004, and continued until she filed the second Form I-485 on October 17, 2007, over three years. Therefore, the period the respondent was out of status far exceeded the allowable 180 days. The motion essentially disagrees with our conclusion, but does not demonstrate any errors of law or fact in our prior decision that would warrant reconsideration.¿FN2º Accordingly, the following order will be entered.¿FN3º
 
ORDER: The motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 5559146 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLANIYI OLADUNJOYE FAJANA
File: AXXX XX0 311 - Baltimore, MD
December 21, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se¿FN1º
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The final administrative order was entered by this Board on December 28, 2009. Thereafter, in a decision dated August 19, 2010, the Board denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. On October 15, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. With certain exceptions not pertinent here, the respondent is limited to one motion to reopen and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent’s motion to reopen is therefore barred by the time limitations applicable to motions. Furthermore, to the extent the respondent seeks reconsideration of the Board’s August 19, 2010, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider, the respondent’s motion is barred by regulation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). Nonetheless, to the extent the respondent’s motion rests on previously raised issues related to the alleged ineffective assistance of his first counsel, Irene M. Recio, the respondent has not identified any error of fact or law in the Board’s previous detailed decision that would alter the outcome. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider).
 
Moreover, notwithstanding the respondent’s alleged “confusion” over the date of his scheduled hearing, the respondent does not dispute that the Immigration Judge personally advised the respondent that he was required to appear on September 3, 2008, for his next scheduled hearing. Nor does the respondent specifically aver either that he was unable to understand the Immigration Judge’s direction or that his first counsel, Irene M. Recio, failed to advise the respondent that he must appear as directed. That the respondent apparently believed that he also had a hearing scheduled in April of 2008, does not alone support the respondent’s assertion that his first counsel never properly advised him that he was required to appear for his September 3, 2008, scheduled hearing, particularly in light of the respondent’s first counsel’s assertion that she did, in fact, advise the respondent that he was required to appear as scheduled. Under the circumstances, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated the required prejudice stemming from his second counsel’s, John D. Lee, alleged action or inaction on the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim against the respondent’s first counsel, Irene M. Recio. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
*2 On this record, the respondent has provided us with no adequate reason to overlook the untimeliness of his motion. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

Dillard as the attorney of record for the respondent as she has not submitted the required Form EOIR-27 (Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Board of Immigration Appeals). Because of this, we will send a courtesy copy of the decision to Nicole C. Dillard, Esquire.

IN RE: KELVYN BATIA-SANTOS A.K.A. LEONARDO BATIA A.K.A. KELVIN BATIA

Oriosto Medrano Santana, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B))
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s August 11, 2010, decision finding him removable from the United States, as charged. The appeal will be sustained and removal proceedings terminated.
 
The respondent is a 28-year-old native and citizen of the Dominican Republic who entered the United States on February 16, 1996, as a lawful permanent resident. The respondent was convicted in the Chelsea District Court, Chelsea, Massachusetts, on June 2, 2005, of distribution of a controlled substance (cocaine) in violation of Chapter 94C, Section 32A(a) of the Massachusetts General Laws. He was sentenced to 1 year in the House of Correction. He maintained the Immigration Judge should have granted him a continuance to have his conviction vacated under Padilla v. Kentucky, ___ U.S. ___, 130 S. Ct. 1473 (2010). The respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel in that he allegedly was not informed of the immigration consequences of his conviction. See Padilla v. Kentucky, supra (counsel may provide ineffective assistance to a criminal defendant by failing to advise him of the possibility of deportation as the result of a guilty plea).
 
The respondent states the Immigration Judge erred because he did not grant him a continuance until the Massachusetts State court decided his motion to vacate his conviction. The Immigration Judge declined to grant him a continuance. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. On appeal he has shown that his motion to withdraw his guilty plea and vacate the judgment of conviction was granted by the Chelsea District Court on September 15, 2010. He has requested termination of proceedings or a remand for the Immigration Judge to terminate proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded. As the respondent’s conviction is no longer final, removal proceedings must be terminated.
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained; removal proceedings are terminated.
 
Lauri S. Filppu

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIEL ANTONIO VELASOUEZ-MENDIZABAL
File: AXXX XX3 694 - Las Vegas, NV
December 16, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Peter L. Ashman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Peter Eitel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of Guatemala and a lawful permanent resident of the United States. On August 26, 2010, he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia after failing to appear at a hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on September 22, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied it on October 12, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be sustained.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that his failure to appear at the hearing was caused by “exceptional circumstances.” He asserts that his former counsel was ineffective by not notifying him of his hearing. The Board need not resolve whether the respondent’s failure to appear at his hearing was caused by ineffective assistance of counsel because we will reopen these proceedings sua sponte.
 
In Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), we held that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship. Herein, we consider that the respondent has resided in the United States as a lawful permanent resident since 1983. He further appears to have been eligible to apply at his hearing for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent additionally indicates in his affidavit that his mother, grandmother, and three siblings are United States citizens. He further states that he has been involved in a romantic relationship for seven years with a citizen of this country. Thus, the respondent appears to lack any motivation to avoid his hearing and abandon his opportunity to remain in the United States as a lawful permanent resident.
 
We further observe that the respondent appears to have been diligent regarding maintaining his immigration status in the United States. He hired an attorney to represent him before the Immigration Court, and states that he traveled from Las Vegas to an earlier hearing at Houston, Texas, prior to venue being changed. We further note the respondent’s assertions in his affidavit alleging that he diligently maintained contact with his former attorney regarding his obligation to appear for a hearing before the Immigration Court. He also filed his motion to reopen less than a month after being ordered removed from the United States in absentia.
 
*2 In addition, the Board has not overlooked that the respondent was notified of his hearing. It is undisputed that the respondent’s Notice of Hearing was received by the respondent’s former attorney. In addition, we note that both the respondent’s former attorney and the Immigration Court mailed a copy of the Notice of Hearing to the address that the respondent acknowledges in his affidavit to be his correct address. The respondent’s claim to have not received either notice is therefore questionable.
 
Notwithstanding, we will in the exercise of discretion reopen these proceedings sua sponte to allow the respondent an opportunity for a hearing. We accord significant weight in this determination to the respondent’s lengthy period in the United States as a lawful permanent resident. We also accord substantial weight to his diligence in filing his motion to reopen and his lack of incentive to avoid his hearing.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and these proceedings are reopened and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Carol King
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN ENRIQUEZ GARCIA A.K.A. JUAN GABRIEL GARCIA
File: AXXX XX3 506 - Los Angeles, CA
December 14, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas J. Tarigo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 6, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he did not receive ineffective assistance of counsel at his prior hearing. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462,464-65 (BIA2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&NDec. 493 (BIA2008).
 
Initially, we note that the respondent’s September 17, 2009, motion to reopen is untimely, inasmuch as the respondent failed to file his motion to reopen within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s October 12, 2004, decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). Furthermore, while we recognize that the respondent has complied with the requirements outlined by the Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we find that the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance. An attorney’s tactical decisions in the preparation and presentation of an alien’s case do not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel See Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986). Respondent’s counsel advised the respondent to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure, given the unlikelihood that the respondent would be granted cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act with his extensive criminal history, including convictions for petty theft, possession of a controlled substance, grand theft, spousal battery, vandalism, and inflicting corporal injury on a spouse. Under these circumstances, we find that the respondent has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted in this matter. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Jean C. King
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 5635168 (BIA)

2010 WL 5635169 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIZABETH ESPINOZA YTAC
File: AXXX XX0 402 - Los Angeles, CA
December 14, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luisito L. Lopez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 23, 2009, decision denying her motion to reopen her deportation proceedings following the Immigration Judge’s November 20, 2009, order granting the respondent voluntary departure.¿FN1º See section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not satisfy the threshold requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (I.J. at 2-3). Although the respondent submitted a detailed letter of complaint addressed to her former counsel and a complaint form submitted to the disciplinary board of the State Bar of California, she did not submit an affidavit (I.J. at 2). Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. The Lozada requirements are not “rigidly” enforced by the United States Court of Appeal for the Ninth Circuit, but the Court has “never excused a petitioner’s failure to provide an affidavit where, as here, the facts underlying the petitioner’s claim were not ‘plain on the face of the administrative record.”’ Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597 (9th Cir. 2004) (internal citation omitted). Because the record does not show “a clear and obvious case” of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent was not excused from meeting the formal requirements of Lozada. Castillo- Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (excusing compliance with Lozada where prior counsel did not timely file applications for relief but told the alien that the applications had been filed and the alien would have been prima facie eligible for relief).
 
Even if the respondent had substantially complied with the Lozada procedural factors, she did not establish that she suffered prejudice as a result of her former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). First, the respondent claims that her former counsel improperly advised her to file a Form I-485 (Application to Register Permanent Residence or Adjust Status) and that the denial of the Form I-485 led to her placement in removal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 7-8, 10). The respondent has not established, however, that these actions of her former counsel made the removal proceedings themselves unfair. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 974 (9th Cir. 2004) (noting that “¿rºemoval proceedings do not become constitutionally unfair simply because they are precipitated in part by an attorney’s advice instead of general INS delay, or because the illegal alien might believe that he could avoid detection until eligible for another form of relief”), amended on other grounds by 404 F.3d 1105 (9th Cir. 2005).
 
*2 Second, the respondent claims to have suffered prejudice because her former counsel did not inform the Immigration Judge of the respondent’s efforts to dissolve her current marriage and marry her United States citizen boyfriend. The Immigration Judge, however, indicated that he would not have granted a continuance on that basis (I.J. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356 (BIA 1983). Moreover, as the Immigration Judge found and the respondent admits on appeal, voluntary departure was the only relief available to the respondent at the time of the hearing (I.J. at 3; Respondent’s Br. at 8). The respondent has not demonstrated that her former counsel’s failure to inform the Immigration Judge of the respondent’s marital plans may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785 (9th Cir. 2005). Accordingly, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s grant of voluntary departure was automatically terminated and transmuted to a removal order when the respondent filed a post-order motion to reopen during the period allowed for voluntary departure. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3)(iii) (2010).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARINA TEOLINDA OLIVERA-RIOS

December 14, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alina Cruz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision dated March 19, 2010. To the extent that the respondent argues that we erred in finding that the respondent first raised her ineffective assistance claim while her case was before us on her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 26, 2008, decision, we acknowledge that such a claim was raised prior to our May 3, 2007, decision remanding the record to the Immigration Judge for a new decision. However, we find no error in our determination that she did demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance. Our last order addressed her arguments, made with the assistance of her new counsel, regarding her eligibility for adjustment under the Cuban Adjustment Act or a family member visa petition in our last order. The respondent does not allege error with respect to our determination that she is not eligible for adjustment under the Cuban Adjustment Act and we remain unpersuaded by her renewed arguments in the motion that she is eligible to adjust her status based on a family visa petition. We also note that the materials in the record pertaining to the ineffective assistance of counsel claim indicate that the respondent did not retain the attorney against whom her ineffective assistance claim is alleged until February 2005, well after the Board affirmed, in the separate visa petition proceedings, the district director’s denial of the petition filed by her present husband based on a finding that the section 204(c) bar applies. She may not relitigate the visa petition denial in the removal proceedings. Further, the respondent has not provided any controlling case law holding that a section 212(i) waiver is available in the circumstances presented here. Her continued reliance on Virk v. INS, 295 F.3d 1055 (9th Cir. 2002), is unavailing. The respondent’s proceedings fall within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, not the Ninth. Further, that case concerned an alien who had been admitted as a lawful permanent resident and was seeking a waiver of removability under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(H), and is inapposite. Accordingly, we deny the motion to reconsider.
 
David B. Holmes

2010 WL 5635164 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO CASILLAS-MEDINA A.K.A. RICHARD MEDINA CASILLAS A.K.A. RICARDO CASILLAS
File: AXXX XX8 478 - Los Angeles, CA
December 8, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eduardo Lachica Encinas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rubab Z. Razvi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on April 27, 2001, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on October 8, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied it on January 6, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The Immigration Judge determined that she lacked jurisdiction to reopen these proceedings. According to the Immigration Judge, the respondent was earlier removed from the United States, and the government reinstated the prior order of removal on December 15, 2008, when the respondent attempted to reenter the United States (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge thus determined that she did not have jurisdiction to reopen these proceedings.
 
The respondent does not contest on appeal the Immigration Judge’s factual finding that the respondent was removed from the United States, and the government had reinstated the prior removal order. The record also contains documentation corroborating the prior removal and reinstatement. See Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979 (9th Cir. 2007). The respondent further does not specifically address the Immigration Judge’s determination that she lacked jurisdiction to reopen the proceedings. Instead, the respondent argues that “exceptional circumstances” prevented him from appearing at his hearing. He asserts that his former counsel instructed him not to appear because the case would be rescheduled. He claims that his failure to appear at his hearing was caused by ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008), the Board held that it and the Immigration Judge lacked authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) to reopen removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings—whether on motion of an alien or sua sponte—if the alien has departed the United States after those administrative proceedings have been completed. However, the Board later held that an alien’s departure from the United States while under an outstanding order of deportation or removal issued in absentia does not deprive the Immigration Judge of jurisdiction to entertain a motion to reopen to rescind the order if the motion is premised upon lack of notice. See Matter of Bulnes, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 Herein, the respondent admits receiving notice of the hearing. The respondent’s claim for reopening made subsequent to removal based on “exceptional circumstances” does not fall within the scope of Matter of Bulnes, supra. We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that she lacked jurisdiction to reopen these proceedings.
 
The Immigration Judge additionally found that the respondent did not establish “exceptional circumstances” warranting reopening even assuming that she had jurisdiction to grant the motion. There is no dispute that the motion is untimely because it was filed more than 180 days after the removal order. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The Immigration Judge also declined to equitably toll the 180-day period for seeking reopening.
 
The Immigration Judge mentioned several reasons for denying equitable tolling of the reopening period. Most significant to the Board is the lack of due diligence exercised by the respondent. According to the respondent’s affidavit, he learned on May 1, 2001, that he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. The respondent states that his former attorney assured him that he would rectify the situation. He additionally states in his affidavit that he “from time to time” called his former attorney after 2001 to ascertain the progress of the effort to have his case reopened. The respondent provides scant detail regarding the frequency of his inquiries. He states that he was finally told on January 5, 2009, that his former counsel did not file the motion to reopen.
 
The respondent did not exercise due diligence in maintaining his immigration status during the many years that elapsed between his order of removal and January 5, 2009. That period of inaction is excessive. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002). We will not equitably toll the period for seeking reopening until January 5, 2009. The respondent’s motion would nevertheless be untimely even if the Board found it appropriate to equitably toll the 180-day period to January 5, 2009. As mentioned, the respondent filed his motion to reopen on October 8, 2009, more than ten months after allegedly learning that his former counsel did not file a motion to reopen on his behalf. That additional delay is another reason for finding that due diligence was not exercised herein. The respondent may have been a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, it was incumbent on him to diligently raise the issue with the Immigration Judge. He did not do so. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Jean C. King
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 5635164 (BIA)

2010 WL 5173941 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JERMAINE WILLIAMS
File: AXXX XX7 893 - New York, NY
December 3, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, who remained in the United States without authorization, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 15, 2010.¿FN1º The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and found him ineligible for relief from removal.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’ findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.l(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent has been in removal proceedings since April 2009. He married a United States citizen on August 27, 2009, and they have a child in common. Because of her citizenship, the respondent’s wife could have, at any time since they married, file a visa petition (Form I-130) on his behalf. At the hearing below, the respondent was represented by Accredited Representative Reverend Robert Vitaglione. The Accredited Representative requested, and was granted, continuances to enable the respondent’s wife to file the visa petition on the respondent’s behalf. At the final hearing, on July 15, 2010, the Accredited Representative advised the Immigration Judge that the respondent had told him that the respondent’s wife had failed to file a visa petition on his behalf because she lacked the funds. See Tr. at 45-46. Based on that, the Immigration Judge found the relief abandoned, found the respondent removable, and ordered the respondent removed. See Tr. at 47-48.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims that it is the Accredited Representative’s fault that the visa petition was never filed. First, we note that the respondent was present when the Accredited Representative told the Immigration Judge that the respondent had told him that the visa petition had not been filed because the respondent’s wife lacked the funds. And the respondent did not object at the time and tell the Immigration Judge that the Accredited representative’s statement was not true. See Tr. at 46.
 
Moreover, the respondent has not fulfilled the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent has not shown evidence that he has notified the former Accredited Representative of the allegation of ineffective assistance, enabling the former Accredited Representative to respond to the allegations. See Matter of Grijalva, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 We also note that the respondent does not indicate on appeal that his United States citizen wife has, since the hearing, filed a visa petition on his behalf.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Carol King
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the Immigration Judge’s Order indicates that the respondent waived appeal. However, the hearing transcript reflects that the respondent indicated a desire to appeal the decision. See Tr. at 49. We also note that the respondent’s appeal was untimely. It was due on August 16, 2010, but was not received at this Board until August 20, 2010. Because the respondent is pro se, and because of the confusion wrought by the Immigration Judge’s order, we will take this appeal on certification. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c).

2010 WL 5173948 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAKUB KSIAZEK
File: AXXX XX3 935 - Chicago, IL
December 2, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Baldini-Potermin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mirta J. Woodall
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen the Board’s June 9, 2010, decision summarily dismissing his appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his application for cancellation of removal. He also seeks a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion and stay request are granted, and the record remanded.
 
The respondent claims in his motion that his former representative, Mr. McGonnagle, provided him with ineffective assistance by failing to submit relevant evidence relating to his positive equities and the associated decision whether a discretionary grant relief was warranted. He further alleges error in that former counsel’s failure to provide any reasons or bases for the appeal in either the Notice of Appeal or an appeal brief resulted in a summary dismissal. Mr. McGonnagle also reportedly erred by advising the respondent to plead guilty regarding a controlled substance charge despite his claim that he did not commit the offense. He contends these errors prejudiced him by preventing his claim from being fully presented, causing him to accept a criminal plea that adversely affected his immigration case, and resulting in the denial of his application for cancellation.
 
The record reflects that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and has shown that Mr. McGonnagle’s actions caused him prejudice. Surganova v. Holder, 612 F.3d 901, 907 (7th Cir. 2010); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Not only has the respondent shown that a bar complaint has been filed against former counsel with appropriate notice given, but he has also filed a receipt of the same from the bar authority (Tabs A, B; Suppl. Tab B). No response has been received from Mr. McGonnagle. The respondent has also shown that a post-conviction petition regarding the controlled substance offense has been filed. Given this record, we find it appropriate to grant the respondent’s motion and request for a stay of removal. The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 5173948 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 4971012 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WENGANG GUO
File: AXXX XX3 641 - New York, NY
November 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Chunyu Jean Wang, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s August 26, 2010, decision to deny his untimely motion to reopen. On October 17, 2003, the final administrative order in the respondent’s removal proceeding was entered by the Immigration Judge. On August 3, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel and new relief. On August 26, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied his motion finding the respondent failed to timely file, did not comply with proper ineffective assistance of counsel requirements, did not show due diligence.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and that the prior Immigration Judge was biased and hostile towards his case. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
“¿Aº party may file only one motion to reopen deportation or exclusion proceedings (whether before the Board of the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent did not file his motion to reopen within 90 days of the final administrative decision, nor did he file it on or before September 30, 1996. As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent filed his motion approximately 6-years late (I.J. at 2). As such, we agree with the Immigration Judge that his motion to reopen the final administrative order is time-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The 90-day filing deadline may be equitably tolled when the motion to reopen is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154 (2d Cir. 2006). The respondent has argued that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and has complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In order to prevail on such a motion the movant must show that he exercised “due diligence in pursuing the case during the period ¿heº seeks to toll.” Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must also meet the procedural requirements set out in Matter of Lozada, supra. Finally, the movant must establish that she suffered prejudice because of her attorney’s actions or failure to act. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638.
 
*2 No matter how ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006). The filing deadline for motions to reopen will be equitably tolled until the ineffective assistance is or should have been discovered by a reasonable person in the situation. See Cekic v. INS, supra. The party seeking such tolling bears the burden of proving that he was, in fact, diligent. See id.
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not adequately comply with the Matter of Lozada, supra, requirements as there was no proof a complaint was filed with the disciplinary committee or his prior counsel (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent was present at the merits hearing, aware and cognizant of the adverse determination rendered against him in 2003, and acknowledged that he wanted to appeal the decision at that time (I.J. at 2-3). Although he allegedly lost contact with his attorney, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to present sufficient evidence that he attempted to write or contact his attorney or the Board of Immigration Appeals to inquire about the status of his case (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge held that even assuming arguendo there was ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent did not demonstrate due diligence by waiting 6-years to file his motion. See Cekic v. INS, supra. As the respondent filed the current motion to reopen approximately 6-years late, the respondent has not shown the requisite due diligence. Therefore, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the time bar for filing a motion to reopen may not be equitably tolled in his case (I.J. at 2). See Cekic v. INS, supra; see also Iavorski v. INS, supra.
 
Finally, the respondent argues that the prior Immigration Judge was biased and had a predisposition towards a disfavorable ruling in his case (Respondent’s Br. at 4-5). Although the Immigration Judge was dissatisfied with the respondent’s non-responsive and evasive answers, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the prior Immigration Judge’s behavior or statements on this record do not support a finding that he was biased or had pre-judged the case. Moreover, we note that the respondent’s disagreement with the ultimate outcome of the Immigration Judge’s decision is insufficient to demonstrate that the outcome was the result of bias or pre-judgement rather than his view of the facts and evidence presented. Consequently, we find that the respondent was not deprived of a full and fair hearing and received his due process. In sum, the respondent has failed to raise any argument on appeal warranting disturbing the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4971012 (BIA)
End of Document

REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 On May 20, 2010, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding him to be an arriving alien who was inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii), for making a false claim of United States citizenship. On August 18, 2010, the respondent filed a “Motion to Reopen Proceedings for Entry of New, Duplicate Decision Due to Ineffective Assistance of Counsel.” The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
We have considered the arguments presented by the parties in light of Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (finding that the Board has discretion to consider claims of ineffective assistance of counsel based on the conduct of counsel that occurred after a final order of removal has been entered). We have also considered this matter in light of Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (concluding that strict compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), is not required when ineffectiveness is obvious), and Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003) (waiving strict Lozada compliance when the attorney promptly acknowledged the error and there was no suggestion of collusion). We will exercise our discretion and will grant the respondent’s motion. Our decision of May 20, 2010, will be vacated and a final order in this matter will be issued as of this date, incorporating by reference the text of the attached vacated order.

2010 WL 4971042 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DESMOND DE RULLIN FITZ MAURICE WHYTE
File: AXXX XX5 760 - Boston, MA
November 22, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: 212(c) waiver
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision of July 7, 2010, pretermitting his request for a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c))(repealed 1996) and ordering him removed from the United States. The respondent has also submitted a motion to terminate his removal proceedings and a motion to reconsider and reopen his proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed. The motions will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard, and review all other issues, including whether the parties have met their burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent was convicted of an aggravated felony drug trafficking crime based upon his 1991 guilty plea for violating Connecticut General Statutes (CGS) § 21a-277a (Possession with Intent to Distribute Narcotics), for which he was sentenced to 12 years imprisonment (I.J. at 2, 4; Notice to Appear). The Immigration Judge also concluded that the respondent is not eligible for a 212(c) waiver because he served more than 5 years in prison for his 1991 aggravated felony conviction. Alternatively, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k)(2), the Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for 212(c) relief because he illegally returned to the United States after being issued a final order of removal. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that his conviction constitutes an aggravated felony. The respondent also asserts that the Immigration Judge applied the wrong legal precedent to conclude that he is ineligible to apply for nunc pro tunc consideration of his 212(c) waiver application.¿FN1º
 
While we disagree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is ineligible for a §212(c) waiver due to his prior deportation and re-entry, we agree with his conclusion that the respondent is ineligible due to his aggravated felony conviction and time served. The Immigration Judge applied the modified categorical approach to determine whether the respondent’s conviction constitutes an aggravated felony (I.J. at 4). The Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s plea colloquy, which shows the criminal court judge clearly informed the respondent that he was “charged with Possession of Cocaine with Intent to Sell” in violation of CGS § 21a-277a (Superior Court transcript of State of Connecticut v. Desmond Whyte, dated July 23, 1991 at p. 2). See Shepard v. United States, 544 U.S. 13, (2005) (to determine whether a guilty plea to an offense under a nongeneric statute necessarily admitted elements of the generic offense it is proper to consider a transcript of colloquy between judge and defendant). As such, we disagree with the respondent’s argument on appeal that his conviction did not list a “specific/particular substance,” and his assertion that the Immigration Judge relied on improper evidence to conclude that his conviction constituted a drug trafficking aggravated felony (Respondent’s Brief at 4). Sections 101(a)(43)(B); 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ l101(a)(43)(B); 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II). The respondent also argues that “there is nothing in the guilty plea transcripts indicating that he … ‘admitted’ that he possessed ‘cocaine with intent to sell,”’ and he asserts the Connecticut statutory definition of sale as applied to illegal drug transactions is “much broader than the common definition of sale as an ‘exchange of an object for a value”’ (Respondent’s brief at 5-6). However, the respondent’s plea colloquy repeatedly affirms his intent to sell the cocaine that was in his possession and the proffered facts indicate that he was involved in selling cocaine for money (see Superior Court transcript of State of Connecticut v. Desmond Whyte, dated July 23, 1991, at pp. 2, 5-6, 12-13).
 
*2 Turning to the respondent’s request for a 212(c) waiver, he argues that the Immigration Judge erred by not applying the authority of the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit to his case. We disagree. Once a proper venue for a removal hearing has been determined, an Immigration Judge should analyze removability and relief issues using only the decisions of the circuit in which he or she sits (as well as decisions of the Supreme Court and this Board) as binding authority, since it is to that circuit that any appeal from a final order of removal must be taken. Section 242(b)(2) of the Act. The venue for the respondent’s proceedings was Boston, Massachusetts, under the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the First Circuit. Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent is ineligible for 212(c) relief because he accrued more than 5 years in prison based upon an aggravated felony conviction. Pereira v. Gonzales, 417 F.3d 38, 42-45 (1st Cir. 2005) (an alien continues to accrue time toward the five-year bar to section 212(c) relief after the issuance of a final order of removal, even if based upon an erroneous retroactive application of AEDPA). See also 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(f)(4)(i). The decision of the federal District Judge dismissing the indictment of the respondent for illegal re-entry after deportation may have had the effect of voiding the final administrative order of deportation represented by the Board’s decision of April 26, 2000. However, jurisdiction over this case now lies in the First Circuit due to the filing of a superceding Notice to Appear on February 12, 2010.
 
The respondent’s motions primarily reiterate the arguments he addressed in his appellate brief. Furthermore, the district court decision cited by the respondent in his motions and appellate brief is not binding upon this Board. See Matter of K-S-, 20 I&N Dec. 715 (BIA 1993). Regarding the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider and Reopen at pp. 4-5), there is no evidence that the respondent’s counsel performed in a manner that would justify remanding this case to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to terminate is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reconsider and reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In 1996, an Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for a section 212(c) waiver based upon section 440(d) of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (“AEDPA”) (I.J. at 2). Subsequently, it was determined that restrictions on section 212(c) added by the AEDPA did not apply retroactively. INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001).

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEJANDRO MENDIVEL-VIZCARRA

November 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alan J. Pollack, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on June 7, 2010, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s September 3, 2008, decision finding the respondent removable as charged and ineligible to adjust his status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1255, in the current proceedings. On July 1, 2010, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen and reconsider based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. For the reasons set out below, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
We reject the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim. An ineffective assistance of counsel claim must demonstrate that the alien has been prejudiced by the former counsel’s poor performance. See Rranci v. Attorney General of the United States, 540 F.3d 165, 175-76 (3d Cir. 2008) (stating that the alien “must show that there was a reasonable likelihood that the result would have been different if the error¿sº … had not occurred.”). Consequently, even if the respondent’s former counsel has been disciplined by state bar authorities or may have provided incompetent representation, the respondent is nonetheless required to demonstrate prejudice at the hearing below or on appeal. However, in the case at bar, the respondent cannot demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions or inactions because he is not eligible for adjustment of status as he entered the United States without being admitted or paroled. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(b)(3). Further, even assuming arguendo the respondent is an arriving alien based on his subsequent parole,¿FN1º the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”), not the Immigration Judge, has jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s adjustment of status application, see 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(i)-(ii),¿FN2º and the Board generally lacks the authority to reopen the proceedings of aliens under final orders of removal who seek to pursue relief for which the Board has no jurisdiction. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009). Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for a stay is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has not supported his claim that he is an arriving alien because he was paroled after entering the United States without inspection. A case previously cited by the respondent, Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98 (3d 2008), is distinguishable from the case at bar because in that case the alien left the United States pursuant to an advance parole.

2

Because the respondent was not granted advance parole, he does not qualify for an exception wherein the Immigration Judge would have jurisdiction over an arriving alien. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(ii)(B), (D).

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARLEEN PATRICIA JACKSON
File: AXXX XX9 262 - Buffalo, NY
November 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se¿FN1º
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has had a final order of removal since 1999. Many years later, the respondent requested reopening based on her marriage to a United States citizen spouse. Among other arguments, the respondent alleged that her former attorneys and a nonattorney had provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied reopening on a number of grounds. The respondent filed a timely Notice of Appeal. On May 11, 2009, the Board dismissed the appeal. The respondent filed a timely petition for review, which has been stayed pending resolution of the present motion.
 
On March 16, 2010, over 10 months after the Board’s May 2009 decision, the respondent filed the present motion to reopen. The motion is time and number-barred. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent contends that the time and number-bars should be equitably tolled because the attorney who filed the motion to reopen provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion shall be denied.
 
The respondent has the burden to demonstrate that she exercised due diligence during the “entire” time she seeks to have tolled, which “includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed. Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008). See also Wang v. BIA, supra at 716 (“Wang has failed to satisfy his burden of establishing due diligence..”); Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517 (2d Cir. 2007)(alien must demonstrate that he exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006)(“¿Nºo matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.”).
 
According to PACER, current counsel filed a Notice of Appearance with the Second Circuit on July 30, 2009. The present motion was not filed until March 16, 2010, and neither counsel nor the respondent has provided a sufficient explanation as to why the motion was filed over 7 months later. Therefore, due diligence has not been demonstrated.
 
The other arguments raised involve reconsideration of our May 2009 decision, but the respondent did not file a timely motion to reconsider. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). We decline, moreover, to exercise our sua sponte authority as the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances justifying the exercise of our discretionary authority to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion shall be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent is considered pro se because her counsel did not submit a Notice of Entry of Appearance on Form EOIR-27. Rather, counsel for the respondent filed a Form EOIR-28 with the original motion, and then mistakenly refiled a Form EOIR-28 after being contacted by the Clerk’s office. Since the Clerk’s office acknowledged counsel’s appearance, a courtesy copy of this decision shall be mailed to Frederick P. Korkosz, Esquire, Pearson & Korkosz, 750 Broadway, Albany, NY 12207-2331.
