APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of Bolivia, move to reopen.¿FN1º 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). On October 28, 2009, we dismissed the appeal of the December 11, 2007, Immigration Judge order denying a motion to reopen. The facts argued in the instant motion are virtually the same as those argued before the Immigration Judge, i.e., that the respondent filed for adjustment of status using an approved Petition for Alien Worker (Form I-140). For an unknown reason, prior counsel, Attorney Amelia Ramer, while representing the respondents before the Immigration Judge, withdrew the application for adjustment of status (Form I-245) and accepted voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.§ 1229c(a).
 
The respondent argues ineffective assistance of counsel, but against two people who represented her before Ms. Ramer (see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen filed November 25, 2009 at the Board (“the instant motion”) at 2). The instant motion requests an opportunity to pursue adjustment of status. However, all of the proffered documents pre-date our prior decision. We considered the ineffective assistance of counsel argument in our final decision, in which we concluded that the Immigration Judge was correct in finding that there was no egregious conduct on the part of counsel that would warrant reopening.
 
Nothing has been presented in the instant motion to reopen to cause us to revisit or reverse our prior decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. No new, material, previously unavailable evidence has been proffered that would warrant reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered;
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.¿FN2º
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2010 WL 4972439 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRIDAY EMIKO OYIBO ITIE
File: AXXX XX4 634 - Dallas, TX
November 16, 2010
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vincent P. Martin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on July 30, 2010, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his deportation proceedings that were conducted in absentia on May 9, 1994. Specifically, noting that the record contains several notices of hearing that were mailed by certified mail to the last address provided by the respondent, the Board concluded that the respondent has not overcome the presumption of effective delivery by substantial and probative evidence of improper delivery. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27, 37 (BIA 1995). On September 1, 2010, the respondent filed a request for reconsideration. The motion will be denied.
 
By notice dated August 27, 2010, the respondent was notified that his motion to reconsider was being rejected because it did not contain the required fee or Fee Waiver Request form (Form EOIR-26A), and the motion was not accompanied by a completed Notice of Entry of Appearance (Form-EOIR-27). The respondent did not resubmit his motion until September 1, 2010. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is untimely.¿FN1º
 
Nevertheless, to the extent the respondent’s motion rests on previously raised issues, the respondent has not identified any issue that was not considered in the Board’s previous detailed decision, nor has he identified any error of fact or law in that decision that would alter the outcome. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). Notably, although the respondent’s notices of hearing were ultimately returned as unclaimed, they were properly sent by certified mail to the last known address provided by the respondent (Notice of Hearing dated March 21, 1994, and corresponding envelope and certified mail receipt; Notice of Hearing dated April 11, 1994, and corresponding envelope and certified mail receipt). Further, the record contains no evidence indicating that the respondent ever notified the Immigration Court of any change in his address since service of the Order to Show Cause in 1994, which the respondent does not deny receiving. See Matter of Grijalva, supra. Under the circumstances, we do not find the respondent’s assertion that he did not reside at the address to which his notices of hearings were sent alone sufficient to demonstrate that the notices of hearings were sent to the wrong address. See, e.g., Joshi v. Ashcroft, 389 F.3d 732, 735 (7th Cir. 2004) (stating that “a bare, uncorroborated, self-serving, denial of receipt, even if sworn, is weak evidence”). Nor do we find the respondent’s mere disagreement with or disapproval of the outcome of the Board’s previous detailed decision sufficient to demonstrate either that the Board improperly evaluated or disregarded the facts and evidence presented or committed legal error. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Fred Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

To the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not sufficiently shown patent error or prejudice. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Nor has he complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).

2010 WL 4972448 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SYFUL MILON
File: AXXX XX7 651 - San Antonio, TX
November 16, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amy N. Gell, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The Board issued a decision in this case on June 2, 2010, dismissing the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying a motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent was previously ordered removed in absentia on July 2, 2007. The respondent has filed a timely motion for reconsideration of our June 2, 2010, decision and another motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). A motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied. A motion to reconsider is not a mechanism by which a party may file a new brief before the Board raising additional legal arguments that are unrelated to those issues raised before the Immigration Judge and on appeal. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra. Notwithstanding the respondent’s disagreement with our decision and our evaluation of the evidence, we find no legal or factual defect in our June 2010 decision, based on the record then before us. Consequently, reconsideration is not warranted.
 
The motion to reopen is predicated upon a claim against the respondent’s former attorney. However, the respondent has not shown substantial compliance with the threshold requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). While the respondent in his affidavit states generally that the Lozada requirements have been met, he has not submitted a copy of the letter to his former attorney or a bar complaint. It is especially important to provide proof of compliance with the Lozada requirements where, as in this case, a respondent’s most recent affidavit contradicts information provided in an prior affidavit and he now claims that he was unaware of the contents of the prior affidavit prepared by his former attorney. Therefore, reopening is not warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider our prior decision and the motion to reopen proceedings will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion for reconsideration of the June 2, 2010, decision and the motion to reopen proceedings are denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4972448 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PHLOENPHIT TONCHOMPHU
File: AXXX XX5 856 - Atlanta, GA
November 9, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James Hallagan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kelley N. Sydnor
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of Thailand. She appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 24, 2010. Therein, an Immigration Judge found her removable from the United States pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). The Immigration Judge further denied the respondent’s application for voluntary departure in the exercise of discretion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she was denied due process in these proceedings. The respondent acknowledges that her former counsel conceded that she was removable from the United States pursuant to the aforementioned charge through written pleadings. She contends that her former counsel should not have conceded and the Immigration Judge should not have found her removable pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. The respondent concedes that she entered and was admitted into the United States using a false name.¿FN1º
 
A formal concession of removability made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity during the respondent’s removal hearing and in the respondent’s presence is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377,382 (BIA 1986); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c). There is no basis for the respondent to now challenge the factual findings or legal conclusions of the Immigration Judge that arose from that concession.
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that her former counsel provided her ineffective assistance of counsel. The procedural requirements for proving ineffective assistance of counsel were not satisfied. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).¿FN2º We further do not find that the respondent’s former counsel’s concession to the charge of removability constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel. It appears that former counsel made the tactical decision to admit to a violation of section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act rather than requesting a finding of removability pursuant to the more serious charge for violating section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. We do not find that counsel’s actions constituted a dereliction of duty. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998). We therefore do not see a reason to invalidate these proceedings or remand the record for the Immigration Judge to make an additional finding of removability pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
*2 The respondent further indicates on appeal that she desires a remand to file an application before the Immigration Judge for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. The respondent abandoned her opportunity to file those applications because she did not do so at her hearing. She further indicates that she intends to file an application for a visa with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services. However, she has not established her prima facie eligibility for a grant of adjustment of status before the Immigration Judge. We will not remand the proceedings. The Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable and denying her application for voluntary departure is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

1

Presumably, the respondent’s contention is that the Immigration Judge should have found her removable from the United States for fraud or misrepresentation pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i).

2

The respondent’s current counsel’s assertions about the lack of communication between the respondent and prior counsel is not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).

IN RE: HECTOR GARCIA VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX3 439 - San Diego, CA

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas D. Nelson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Grant
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
AMENDED ORDER
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 26, 2008, when we denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen, filed on June 16, 2008. However, the record reflects that the respondent had also filed a motion to reconsider on April 16, 2008, which was pending at the time and which we did not explicitly and separately address in our decision. We are therefore reopening proceedings on our own motion to include an adjudication of the respondent’s motion to reconsider. Moreover, given that the law which served as the basis of our denial of the motion to reopen has since changed, we will vacate our prior order in its entirety and issue the following decision.
 
The respondent’s April 16, 2008, motion to reconsider will be denied. The respondent sought reconsideration of our March 18, 2008, decision denying his December 3, 2007, motion to reopen as untimely. In his motion to reopen, the respondent had argued that sua sponte reopening was warranted based upon the Ninth Circuit’s decision in Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2005), which held that lawful admission and legal permanent residence of a parent could be imputed to a minor child to satisfy the continuous residence requirement for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(2). We declined to reopen sua sponte, given that the case had been published more than 2 years prior to the untimely filing of the motion. We find no reason to disturb that determination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; see Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied, as we find no error in our decision as based upon the record before us at that time.
 
However, the respondent’s June 16, 2008, motion to reopen will be granted. In that motion, the respondent asserts that the failure to timely reopen was due to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. In our August 26, 2008, decision, we denied the motion to reopen, finding that Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, supra, was no longer binding on the Board in light of our explicit disagreement with that case in Matter of Ramirez-Vargas, 24 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 2008) and Matter of Escobar, 24 I&N Dec. 231 (BIA 2007). However, since that time, the Ninth Circuit has reaffirmed its holding in Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, supra, finding the Board’s contrary interpretation to be unreasonable. See Mercado-Zazueta v. Holder, 580 F.3d 1102 (9th Cir. 2009). The Ninth Circuit noted that although agencies retain discretion to determine ambiguous statutes, an agency may not “resurrect a statutory interpretation that a circuit court has foreclosed by rejecting it as unreasonable.” Id. at 1114; see also Matter of Diaz and Lopez, 25 I&N Dec. 188, 190 (BIA 2010) (acknowledging same). Therefore, as the respondent claims to have been residing in the United States since 1992 with his father, who was a lawful permanent resident and is now a United States citizen, he appears to be statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
*2 Although the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) challenges the motion as time-barred and number-barred under the regulations, we find that equitable tolling of the filing deadline is warranted. The respondent asserts that his former counsel erred in failing to file a timely motion to reopen after Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, supra, was issued. We note that the respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. Moreover, we accept the respondent’s rationale for not filing a bar complaint. Former counsel has submitted an affidavit admitting his error, and the respondent’s current attorney attests to the prior attorney’s complete cooperation in remedying the oversight. Moreover, the respondent reports that his former attorney had performed work for the respondent beyond their contract without remuneration (June 16, 2008, Motion to Reopen, 4-7).
 
Therefore, as the respondent has shown that he was “prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error,” the deadline will be equitably tolled and his motion is deemed timely. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003); see also Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1185-86 (9th Cir. 2004). Notably, the DHS has presented no argument opposing equitable tolling. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Board’s August 26, 2008, order is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s April 16, 2008, motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s June 16, 2008, motion to reopen is hereby granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE FELIPE MONTALVO
File: AXXX XX5 057 - Dallas, TX
November 4, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justine M. Daly, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cynthia D. Goodman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, appeals the Immigration Judge’s March 20, 2009, denial of his motion to reconsider a January 5, 2009, Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The respondent sought reopening of an order dated September 10, 2008, in which the Immigration Judge found the respondent had not requested any form of relief, that no relief was available to him, and ordered him removed to Mexico. The respondent waived appeal. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. On January 5, 2009, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because the respondent had not established that he was prima facie eligible for relief. The respondent filed a January 28, 2009, motion to reconsider. Based on the motion to reconsider, the Immigration Judge entered a new decision on March 20, 2009, correcting a legal error. However, the motion was again denied because the respondent failed to demonstrate he was prima facie eligible for relief. We now consider the respondent’s appeal of that decision.
 
A motion to reopen for the purpose of providing the alien an opportunity to apply for any form of discretionary relief will not be granted if it appears that the alien’s right to apply for such relief was fully explained to him or her by the Immigration Judge and an opportunity to apply therefore was afforded at the hearing, unless the relief is sought on the basis of circumstances that have arisen subsequent to the hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent has made no showing of new circumstances that have arisen subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision. However, the respondent argues on appeal that if not for the ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent would have filed the appropriate applications for relief with the Immigration Judge. The respondent further argues that he is prima facie eligible for the relief requested and reopening is appropriate. Respondent’s brief at 15, 17.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998). Based on the documentation presented, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent has met the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 However, the Immigration Judge denied reopening because the respondent had not demonstrated that he was prejudiced as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent was not prima facie eligible for relief and would not have prevailed at a hearing. The respondent argues that he is eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(d)(11) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(d)(11). The respondent concedes that he was convicted on July 18, 2007, in the United States District Court, Western District of Texas, Del Rio Division, for the offense of aiding and abetting the attempted illegal entry of an alien in violation of 8 U.S.C. § 1325(a)(3) and 18 U.S.C. § 2. Based on this conviction, the respondent is removable as an alien who knowingly encouraged, induced, assisted, abetted, or aided any other alien to enter or to try to enter the United States in violation of law pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i). However, the respondent argues that the aliens he was assisting in entering the country were his step children and he is eligible for consideration of a waiver under section 212(d)(11) of the Act. The respondent offers a Declaration and Registration of Informal Marriage, to support his contention that under the laws of Texas he was common law married when he brought his step children into the United States illegally. The Immigration Judge found that the evidence was not sufficient to indicate that a common law marriage existed at the time of the respondent’s July 7, 2007, arrest because although the document states that the marriage began prior to the incident on January 7, 2007, the declaration was not executed until April 7, 2008 (I.J. at 6).
 
The marriage of a man and woman in Texas may be proven by evidence that a declaration of their marriage has been signed. See Texas Family Code section 2.401(a)(1). Furthermore, the Texas courts have found that a declaration of marriage constitutes prima facie proof of the parties’ informal marriage. See Russell v. Russell, 865 S.W. 2d 929, 931 (Tex. 1993); Husband v. Pierce, 800 S.W. 2d 661 (Tex. 1990) (declaration is a prima facie showing of union). Therefore, we find that the Immigration Judge erred in not finding the respondent had provided prima facie evidence of his eligibility for the section 212(d)(11) waiver. The evidence sufficiently demonstrated the respondent had been prejudiced by his former attorney’s ineffective assistance. Based on this evidence, we will remand to the Immigration Judge for initial consideration of the respondent’s eligibility for the claimed waiver.
 
*3 We note that although the respondent has provided sufficient evidence to support reopening, the evidence does not necessarily establish the required family relationship for a section 212(d)(11) waiver. Section 2.401 of the Texas Family Code provides only that a marriage may be proven by evidence of a signed declaration, not that the marriage is definitively established by such a declaration. The Texas courts have held that they are not bound to find a marriage as stated in a declaration when there is evidence to the contrary. See Colburn v. The State of Texas, 966 S. W. 2d 511 (Tex. 1998). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s factual finding that since the declaration of marriage was executed subsequent to the respondent’s arrest for alien smuggling, there is some evidence in the record that does not support an informal marriage was created on January 7, 2007 (I.J. at 5). Therefore, on remand the Immigration Judge will consider additional evidence, make explicit fact finding and conduct additional legal analysis as to whether a common law marriage had been established by July 7, 2007.
 
Furthermore, we note that eligibility for a section 212(d)(11) waiver is not based solely upon the establishment of the claimed relationship. See Matter of Compean, 21 I&N Dec. 51 (BIA 1995). Rather, the respondent must establish that such a grant is for humanitarian reasons, to assure family unity, or is otherwise in the public interest. See section 212(d)(11) of the Act; Matter of Compean, supra.
 
Finally, we find no basis to remand to a different Immigration Judge, as requested by the respondent. We have reviewed the contention by the respondent that the Immigration Judge has demonstrated bias in this matter. This contention, without further support, is insufficient to justify remand to another Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge has conducted these proceedings in a generally fair way, and we see no persuasive evidence of bias on the part of the Immigration Judge, such as to require a remand to a different Immigration Judge. See Matter of G-, 20 I&N Dec. 764, 781 (BIA 1993). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4823003 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: ALICE NYAWIRA KARURI

APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se¿FN1º
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joey L. Caccarozzo
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s December 16, 2008, denial of her motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including any determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of H-L-H- & Z-Y-Z-, 25 I&N Dec. 209, 211 (BIA 2010).
 
On November 7, 2007, the respondent was ordered removed to Kenya after having failed to comply with biometric requirements. She filed a motion to reopen on November 14, 2007, which the Immigration Judge granted on December 3, 2007. On July 17, 2008, the Immigration Judge deemed the respondent’s application for adjustment of status to have been withdrawn, but granted her voluntary departure. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen on November 14, 2008, which the Immigration Judge denied on December 16, 2008. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of this untimely and number-barred motion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
In her second motion to reopen and on appeal, the respondent has alleged the ineffective assistance of two prior counsels. However, in making these allegations, she failed to meet any of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 851F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). That is, she failed, even on appeal, to submit an affidavit detailing the agreements she had with her prior counsels, to assert that the prior counsels have been informed of the allegations leveled against them and been given an opportunity to respond, or to confirm that bar complaints have been filed against the prior counsels, or alternatively, explain why such complaints have not been filed. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639; Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 745-46 (4th Cir. 2006).
 
The respondent also argued on appeal that, in addition to the requirements of Matter of Lozada, the Immigration Judge appeared to require a showing that the prior counsels were not in “good standing” with the Immigration Court (Respondent’s Brief at 4-6; I.J. at 1). However, our reading of the Immigration Judge’s order is not that he added such a requirement for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, but rather that he just noted in passing that both of the prior counsels were generally considered to be in good standing in his court. In any event, whether or not these attorneys were in “good standing” has no bearing on whether the respondent has otherwise fulfilled the Lozada requirements.
 
*2 Further, in the absence of egregious circumstances, an alien is bound by the “reasonable tactical actions” of his or her counsel. Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938 (BIA 1995). Based on the respondent’s allegations, we are unable to conclude that the actions of either of her prior counsels were egregious. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Finally, we conclude that the circumstances of this case do not warrant sua sponte reopening.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

As the attorney’s motion conforms with the requirements Matter of Rosales, 19 I&N Dec. 655 (BIA 1988), we will grant the motion, consider the respondent pro se, and issue a courtesy copy of our decision to Ms. Njogu.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION

Paul S. Zolton, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case is before us pursuant to an order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit granting an unopposed motion to remand the record. In a decision dated October 30, 2009, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal, but failed to directly address the respondent’s motion to remand the record. The respondent has also filed a motion to reopen the proceedings based on the ineffective assistance of previous counsel. The motion will be granted and the record remanded for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
The respondent argues that her previous attorney failed to timely submit evidence that was material to her case. In our previous decision, we noted that this particular document was not important due to some problems with the testimony concerning how it was obtained. The respondent has now submitted additional evidence bolstering the importance of this document and an affidavit from her former attorney stating that he erred in not submitting this document earlier. The respondent has substantially complied with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). In addition to this, the respondent has now also submitted additional support for her claim, some of which was not available at the time of the previous hearing and appears material to her claim.
 
Based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, the new and material evidence the respondent has submitted, as well as, the request of the Fourth Circuit, we will reopen the proceedings and remand the record for the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s statutory’ eligibility for relief and whether or not discretion should be exercised on her behalf.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted and the record remanded for further proceedings.
 
Neil P. Miller

2010 WL 4822996 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA REINOSO-POAQUIZA
File: AXXX XX3 125 - New York, NY
November 3, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anita Delgado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leigh Mapplebeck
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the April 7, 2010, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, was placed in removal proceedings in 1997 and conceded removability (see Exh. 1). On July 8, 1998, the respondent failed to appear at a hearing and was ordered removed in absentia. On October 26, 1998, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings. The respondent’s counsel was subsequently issued notice that the respondent should appear for a hearing on March 30, 1999. On that date, neither the respondent nor her counsel appeared, and another in absentia order was entered.
 
In October 2003, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen asserting that her prior counsel, whose name she could not recall, informed her that she did not need to appear for her removal hearing. She explained her husband was the beneficiary of an approved Form I-140, and she wanted to pursue adjustment of status. On November 3, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion finding that the hearing notice had been properly issued to the respondent’s counsel, and that the respondent did not present evidence to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, including her own statement. The Immigration Judge pointed out that several years had passed since the in absentia hearing, and the respondent knew she had been placed in proceedings.
 
On March 12, 2010, the respondent filed another motion to reopen, this time supported by her own declaration claiming she did not receive notice of her hearing. She reiterated her desire to apply for adjustment of status and submitted supporting documentation. On April 7, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the motion finding that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear.
 
The respondent has filed this appeal. She again claims her prior counsel never advised her of her hearing date, and she because he is deceased, she cannot comply with the documentary requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. She asserts that the 180-day limit for filing a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances should be equitably tolled. She emphasizes that she also seeks reopening based on previously unavailable evidence supporting her eligibility for adjustment of status.
 
We will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. In her 2003 motion, the respondent blamed her failure to appear on her prior counsel, but did not submit evidence which satisfied any of the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She did not adequately remedy this deficiency in her 2010 motion, wherein she provided a statement that she did not receive her notice of hearing but provided no explanation regarding her relationship with prior counsel. Both of the motions were also untimely as they were filed outside of the 180-day limit for motions to reopen based on exceptional circumstances for a failure to appear. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). The respondent’s failure to properly support her motions, as well as her several year delay in filing her first motion without sufficient explanation, preclude reopening under an equitable tolling theory. See Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595 (2d Cir. 2008); Wang v. BIA, 508 F.3d 710, 715 (2d Cir. 2007). Finally, the respondent’s argument, raised for the first time on appeal, that prior counsel is deceased does not excuse her failure to submit adequate documentation earlier in the proceedings. Setting aside the in absentia issues, insofar as the respondent’s motion is based on previously unavailable relief and new evidence, it is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).
 
*2 We conclude that the respondent has not set forth any basis for reopening her removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C. Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2010 WL 4822992 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANDY BALACUIT ALIBANGBANG A.K.A. SANY BALACUIT ALIBANGBANG
File: AXXX XX6 548 - Las Vegas, NV
November 3, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William F. Levings, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christian Parke
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent appealed from the June 30, 2010, oral decision of the Immigration Judge, which found him removable; determined that he did not apply for, and did not demonstrate eligibility for, any relief from removal; and ordered him removed from the United States to the Philippines. While his appeal was pending, he filed a motion to remand. The motion to remand will be granted, and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found that the respondent was removable based on the charge in the Form I-261 charging document, as convicted of an aggravated felony under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), in conjunction with sections 101(a)(43)(G) and 101(a)(43)(U) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(43)(G) and 1101(a)(43)(U). As substantiated by conviction documents, the respondent has a 2006 Nevada conviction upon a guilty plea for conspiracy to commit burglary. For that crime, he was sentenced to imprisonment of 1 year, with imposition of sentence suspended subject to completion of probation, originally. The prison sentence was imposed subsequently, upon probation revocation in 2008. The respondent was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on or about February 10, 1988. The Immigration Judge indicated that the respondent did not wish to apply for any form of removal relief, based on his statements through counsel at his hearing (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 42).
 
On appeal and in his motion to remand, the respondent argues that his conviction is invalid because he entered a defective guilty plea based on ineffective assistance from criminal counsel. However, the fact that he may be pursuing post-conviction relief in the form of a collateral attack on that conviction in state criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506 (BIA 1992). The respondent has presented no evidence with this appeal that any attack on his conviction has resulted in any vacatur. His speculation that his conviction might be invalid does not change the finality for immigration purposes of such conviction, unless and until it were to be overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996).
 
Further, the respondent asserts that he now wishes to apply on remand for withholding of removal under the Act and for protection under the United Nations Convention Against Torture (“CAT”). We observe that he stands convicted of conspiracy to commit burglary. Since the respondent was sentenced to imprisonment of 1 year for his theft offense, he has been convicted of an aggravated felony. As an aggravated felon, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for asylum under the Act. See sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 208(b)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 1158(b)(2)(B)(i). To demonstrate eligibility for withholding of removal under the Act, the respondent would have to establish that, if he were to return to the Philippines, it is more likely than not that he would be subjected to persecution on account of a statutory ground. See section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3). To demonstrate eligibility for CAT protection, the respondent would have to prove that, if removed to the Philippines, he would more likely than not be tortured at the instigation or with the acquiescence (to include the concept of willful blindness) of a public official acting in his or her official capacity. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(c)(2) and 1208.18(a)(1) (2010). He alleges that he fears returning to the Philippines because he believes that his father, who served in the military and as a police officer, was murdered in 2004 by his enemies for political reasons. The respondent has submitted a completed Form I-589 application accompanied by four affidavits, his own September 2010 affidavit and July 2010 affidavits from two relatives and an acquaintance.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent maintains that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney who represented him in his removal proceedings. In his affidavit and motion, he states that his former attorney did not consult with him before telling the Immigration Judge that there was no relief available to him; did not raise his expressed fear of returning to the Philippines with the Immigration Judge; did not submit a brief even after the Immigration Judge afforded him two extra weeks to do so; did not proffer any reason for not submitting the brief; did not visit him in detention; and rarely spoke to him at his hearings. We find that the respondent has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), as he has submitted his own affidavit and a copy of a complaint that was filed with the State Bar of Nevada and also was sent to his prior attorney. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
Under the circumstances of this case, we find it appropriate to grant the motion to remand so that the respondent’s claims for withholding of removal and CAT protection may be evaluated by the Immigration Judge. Since we have granted the motion to remand, we need not reach the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim and need not determine whether he has demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s alleged action or inaction pursuant to Matter of Lozada, supra, as we expect that the respondent will have the opportunity to present fully his withholding and CAT claims, the inaction regarding which underlies his ineffective assistance claim. See Aguilar-Ramos v. Holder, 594 F.3d 701 (9th Cir. 2010). On remand, both the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security may introduce any and all available evidence that is relevant to his eligibility for withholding of removal and CAT protection.¿FN1º
 
Accordingly, we will grant the motion to remand and will remand the record.
 
ORDER: The motion to remand is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings and for the issuance of a new decision, consistent with this opinion.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

To the extent that the respondent references issues related to his bond and custody status, we lack authority in these removal proceedings to address those matters, which must be raised in the context of his separate bond proceedings.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAKLIN ALEXIS RIVERA-RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX8 849 - Chicago, IL
November 2, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marisa Aguilar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order in these proceedings on February 6, 2008. On October 8, 2010, the respondent filed this untimely motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reopening to apply for relief from removal, including adjustment of status as the spouse of a United States citizen.¿FN1º It does not appear that any exception to the filing deadline applies to this motion, however. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). See also Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009) (untimely motions to reopen to apply for adjustment of status generally do not fall within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions for motions to reopen and are generally denied). Nor do we find that equitable tolling of the filing deadline based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is warranted in this case. See Ajose v. Gonzales, 408 F.3d 393, 395 (7th Cir.2005) (holding that the administrative time-limit on motions to reopen is non-jurisdictional and subject to equitable tolling); Pervaiz v. Gonzales, 405 F.3d 488 (7th Cir. 2005) (recognizing equitable tolling in cases of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
The respondent raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, alleging, inter alia, that his former attorney failed to notify him of the Board’s prior order. The respondent, however, has not complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before this Board as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Patel v. Gonzales, 496 F.3d 829 (7th Cir. 2007) (holding that the Board did not abuse its discretion in denying an alien’s motion to reopen where the alien failed to fulfill the Lozada requirements). He has failed, therefore, to set forth a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his former attorney.
 
Finally, we find no exceptional situation present in this case to persuade us to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings on our own motion. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). While we recognize that the respondent’s removal will likely result in hardship to the respondent and his family, we emphasize that we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, “treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.” Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NESTOR ENRIQUE CORTEZ-VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX5 340 - San Antonio, TX
November 2, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher J. Stender, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin Adams
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on June 21, 2005, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on July 8, 2009, and the Immigration Judge denied it on July 22, 2009. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen on May 10, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied it on July 12, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision sets out the history of this case. Underlying both of the respondent’s motions is his claim that he did not receive proper notice of his hearing. The respondent did not give an address at the time his Notice to Appear (NTA) was served upon him, and he did not subsequently provide the Immigration Court with an address. Therefore, the respondent was not entitled to receive actual notice of his hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(5)(B); Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354 (5th Cir. 2009), and the in absentia hearing was appropriate. The respondent emphasizes that he was only 17 years old when the NTA was served. However, the regulations provide that he was old enough to receive service of the NTA. See 8 C.F.R. § 103.5a(c)(ii). Moreover, the respondent was accompanied by his adult sister at the time of service (see Exhs 1, 2). Once served, it became incumbent on the respondent to provide an address where he was able to receive notices. As he did not do so, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish a basis for reopening based on a lack of proper notice of the in absentia hearing.
 
Insofar as the respondent raises issues outside of the notice of his hearing, he faces jurisdictional barriers as he acknowledges being removed from the United States on or about September 27, 2009, pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s June 21, 2005, decision (See R’s 2010 appeal brief at 4; 2010 motion to reopen at 4).¿FN1º See Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008); cf. Matter of Bulnes, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009) (holding that an alien’s departure from the United States while under an outstanding order of deportation or removal issued in absentia does not deprive the Immigration Judge of jurisdiction to entertain a motion to reopen to rescind the order if the motion is premised upon lack of notice).
 
Additionally, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) provides that an alien may file only one motion to seek reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia. This is the respondent’s second motion to reopen. In this regard, even entertaining the respondent’s argument that his first motion was prepared with ineffective assistance of counsel and number and time bars should not be triggered, we respond that he has not satisfied the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 638 (BIA 1988); see also De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879 (5th Cir. 2004); Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001). Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established any basis for reopening his proceedings under the William Wilberfore Trafficking Victims Protection Reauthorization Act of 2008 (TVPRA). Most notably, the respondent was in fact accompanied at time of his 2005 entry by his sister, who had reached the age of majority, and placed in her custody; he was also traveling to see his parents who lived in Georgia. The respondent otherwise cites no specific provision of the TVPRA which would dictate that these proceedings should be reopened. Finally, we find no merit to the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge was obliged to advise the respondent of potential eligibility for relief in the context of denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.11(a), (c) (referring to Immigration Judge’s duties during a hearing).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen Liebowitz

The Immigration Judge did not address this factor in his dismissal of the respondent’s motion.

IN RE: MAURICIO MARTINEZ-CARMONA
File: AXXX XX4 013 - Los Angeles, CA
November 2, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Particia Vargas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer W. Smith
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, timely requests reopening asserting that the ineffective assistance of his former counsel warrants further hearings on the issue of hardship to his qualifying relatives, four United States citizen children, for purposes of cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The motion will be denied.
 
Notwithstanding his assertion that his former attorney failed to submit evidence relating to country conditions in Mexico, the respondent has not proffered additional new evidence satisfying his “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-74 (BIA 1992); Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N 413 (BIA 1996); Matter of Andazola, 23 I&N Dec. 319 (BIA 2002) (describing exceptional and extremely unusual hardship). The respondent’s generalized claim of violence in Mexico does not sufficiently demonstrate hardship that is substantially different from, or beyond, that which would normally be expected upon returning with a parent to the parent’s country of citizenship, which may be a less developed country. See Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001). We acknowledge the present difficult conditions in Mexico, including the widespread violence there. However, the respondent has not sufficiently shown that his children would be left unprotected from harm or violence in Mexico.
 
Under the circumstances, we do not find the respondent’s assertion regarding his former attorney’s alleged action or inaction sufficient to demonstrate prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Nor has the respondent sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
On this record, the respondent has not demonstrated that further hearings are warranted. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

IN RE: GERMAN DOUGLAS CRUZ GUERRERO
File: AXXX XX6 066 - Dallas, TX
October 29, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosalind Kelly, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 25, 2010, decision, ordering him removed. The respondent has also requested that we remand the record based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal will be dismissed as waived and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
The record reflects that the respondent waived appeal. The Immigration Judge asked the respondent’s counsel if appeal was reserved or waived (Tr. at 5). Counsel replied, at first, that “he’s not instructed me one way or the other.” Id. The respondent was asked if he wished to accept the Immigration Judge’s decision or if he wished to appeal, and the respondent replied that “It’s fine. It’s okay” (Tr. at 6). The Immigration Judge then asked respondent’s counsel if he accepted that as a waiver, and respondent’s counsel replied in the affirmative. Id. The Immigration Judge noted at the bottom of his memorandum decision that both parties waived appeal. The respondent asserts on appeal that his waiver of appeal was unknowing and requests a remand. While the respondent submitted an affidavit on appeal, his only statement regarding the waiver is that counsel did not preserve his appeal rights. The respondent does not state that he did not understand that he was waiving appeal when he, his counsel, and the Immigration Judge discussed the issue. Although the respondent claims that his former counsel was ineffective, this claim is unavailing. To establish a meritorious ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must first comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements that he inform former counsel of the allegations he is lodging against him, as well as notify the state bar disciplinary authority. Furthermore, he has not established a factual basis on which we could conclude that the former counsel was ineffective, and he has not established any prejudice from the asserted ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed as waived.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant

IN RE: FLOYD MCAURTHER ANDERSON
File: AXXX XX7 617 - Napanoch, NY
October 29, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jennifer Oltarsh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
C. Cullen Sheppard
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B)º
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B)º
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on August 27, 2010, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely. On September 27, 20010, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider our denial of his motion to reopen. The motion to reconsider will be denied, and the respondent’s stay request also is denied.
 
A motion to reconsider is “a request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change in law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.” Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57 (BIA 2006). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Id According to section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(C), a motion to reconsider must specify the errors of fact or law in a prior Board decision, and it must be supported by pertinent authority. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1).
 
The respondent’s motion does not meet these requirements. Specifically, the respondent has not identified an error of fact or law in our prior ruling. The respondent instead has offered additional information regarding the merits and progress of the post-conviction motion he has filed in his criminal proceedings. To the extent that the respondent’s motion contains new evidence for consideration, the motion is a motion to reopen. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 57-58. The respondent, however, has already filed one motion to reopen, and his current motion was filed well past the time limit for filing a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen therefore is untimely and numerically barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Act.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent has not established that his motion fits within one of the exceptions to the time and number limits. See sections 240(c)(7)(C)(ii), (iii) and (iv) of the Act; see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i) - (iv). Moreover, as we noted in our prior decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the respondent has not claimed that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his immigration proceedings. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000) (holding that the deadline for motions to reopen may be equitably tolled on the basis of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel provided the alien acts with due diligence); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth requirements for establishing entitlement to reopening on the basis of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Finally, the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening of his proceedings is warranted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (indicating that this Board may grant sua sponte reopening in its discretion when exceptional circumstances exist). The respondent claims that he was denied due process due to his criminal attorney’s failure to consider and advise him of the immigration consequences of his guilty pleas. The respondent argues that the guilty pleas made him removable and ineligible for relief from removal, and he asserts that a criminal court is on the verge of overturning his convictions on the basis of Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010). He therefore claims that his proceedings should be reopened and stayed so that he may pursue relief from removal and avoid irreparable harm.
 
The respondent’s assertion that some or all of his convictions might be invalidated in the near future, however, does not alter the fact that his convictions are final for immigration purposes unless and until they are actually overturned by a criminal court. See, e.g., Matter of Ponce de Leon-Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 156-7 (BIA 1996) (finding that the availability of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attack does not affect the finality of a conviction for immigration purposes); see also Montilla v. INS, 926 F.2d 162, 164 (2d Cir. 1991). Neither this Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction to consider a collateral attack on a conviction or to question the validity of a final conviction. See, e.g., Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). Accordingly, as we stated in our earlier ruling, the respondent’s assertion that his convictions could be overturned in the near future, standing alone, does not provide a proper basis for granting his untimely motion to reopen. We therefore deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider, and we deny what we have construed to constitute a motion to reopen as untimely.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: To the extent that the respondent’s motion to reconsider also constitutes a motion to reopen, it is denied as untimely.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s stay request is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4509741 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDWIN EDGARDO MOREIRA LINO A.K.A. EDWIN MOREIRA
File: AXXX XX2 437 - New York, NY
October 28, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hedwin Salmen-Navarro, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
In a decision dated May 12, 2010, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia on April 2, 2008. The respondent has appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a 26-year-old native and citizen of Honduras. He entered the United States on or about February 1993. The Notice to Appear was issued on January 22, 2007. He did not appear at the hearing on April 2, 2008, despite having been in court when the hearing date was set. The respondent filed a motion to rescind the in absentia order and to reopen proceedings on May 3, 2010, over 2 years after the hearing, in order to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for lack of prejudice and lack of due diligence. The Board denied his request for a stay. The respondent was removed on July 12, 2010.
 
Under the regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d), a motion to reopen shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of exclusion, deportation or removal proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States. In Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008), the Board found that a motion to reopen is barred by the regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2, and the Immigration Judge and the Board do not have jurisdiction over the motion, where the alien has left the United States after he is placed into immigration proceedings. Xue Yong Zhang v. Holder, 617 F.3d 650 (2d Cir. 2010) (departure bar limits Board’s sua sponte jurisdiction over motion to reopen); see Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288 (5th Cir. 2009).
 
In Matter of Bulnes-Nolasco, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009), the Board held an alien could file a motion to rescind an in absentia order of exclusion, deportation, or removal to address whether an alien received sufficient notice of an in absentia deportation hearing. See Xue Yong Zhang, supra, at 8 n. 10 (distinguishing Bulnes-Nolasco). Under the statute, an order of removal can be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed at any time, if the alien demonstrated that he did not receive notice. See Contreras-Rodriguez v. United States Attorney General, 462 F.3d 1314 (11th Cir. 2006); 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The Immigration Judge found the respondent received sufficient notice because he was present when the hearing date was set. Further, he did not explain why he did not appear on April 4, 2008, if he thought that was the correct hearing date, and he did not exercise due diligence in filing a motion to reopen 2 years after the Immigration Judge’s decision.¿FN1º
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that he has shown exceptional circumstances prevented him from appearing in that he was subject to ineffective assistance of counsel. He submitted evidence that the organization for which his attorney worked, the International Professional Association, has been sued by the State of New York for fraud and malfeasance. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He also states he has a mental deficiency as the result of a car accident. However, a motion to rescind on account of exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s order. Section 240(b)(5)(c)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(c)(i) and (e)(1); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and he has not shown due diligence. See e.g. Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008) (no due diligence); Wang v. Bill, 508 F.3d 710 (2d Cir. 2007) (same); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006) (same).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The motion does not contain an affidavit or statement from the respondent regarding these events. To the extent that the respondent has concerns over his memory, he has not shown what steps he took to clarify the hearing date, and hence has not made a case that his failure to attend resulted from circumstances “beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).

2010 WL 4500876 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONIO AVILA-CEJA
File: AXXX XX0 041 - San Francisco, CA
October 28, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen Coghlan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 15, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s timely motion to reopen proceedings. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not demonstrate that he received ineffective assistance of counsel or that proceedings should be reopened in order to permit the respondent to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). On appeal, the respondent argues that he is prima facie eligible for cancellation of removal and requests that his proceedings be remanded to the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
As an initial matter, we note that we do not consider evidence first offered on appeal as our review is a review of the record created before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Federenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). The proper time to submit evidence is before an Immigration Judge at an evidentiary hearing. We note that where evidence is new or previously unavailable it may support a remand. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(1) and 1003.2(c)(4). Since the evidence submitted by the respondent could have been submitted with his motion to reopen, we find that remand is not warranted in this matter. Hence, we will consider the respondent’s appeal based upon the evidence in the record at the hearing before the Immigration Judge.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion to reopen did not demonstrate that the respondent’s removal proceedings should be reopened in order to allow the respondent to pursue an application for cancellation of removal. The respondent’s motion was not supported by the proper documentation to establish the respondent’s prima facie eligibility, where the respondent did not submit an application for cancellation of removal or evidence of continuous physical presence, good moral character, absence of a disqualifying conviction, and the requisite hardship to a qualifying relative. Furthermore, the respondent’s motion did not demonstrate that he received ineffective assistance at his hearing before the Immigration Judge. In light of the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4500876 (BIA)

2010 WL 4509785 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KAREN NATALIE LEE
File: AXXX XX4 427 - Miami, FL
October 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sherna G. Spencer, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a female native and citizen of Jamaica, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 2, 2009, denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia on April 15, 2009, and in a decision dated August 27, 2009, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not established that proceedings should be reopened. There is no issue regarding whether the respondent knew that she had a scheduled removal hearing. Thus, the respondent had notice of her hearing and can seek reopening of proceedings solely upon the basis of exceptional circumstances. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In this case, the respondent claims that she failed to appear because she mistakenly relied on the advice of an immigration consultant. However, the respondent does not claim that the consultant held herself out to be an attorney, nor does the respondent claim that she believed the consultant was an attorney. We therefore find that the respondent has not established exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. See Hernandez v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 1014, 1020 (9th Cir. 2008) (noting that an alien’s knowing reliance on a non-attorney’s advice will not support a due process violation); Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that there is a crucial difference between ineffective assistance of counsel and ineffective assistance of an immigration consultant).
 
In addition, even if the respondent believed that the immigration consultant was an attorney or held herself out as such, her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not satisfy the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224, 1225-26 (11th Cir. 2008) (suggesting that Lozada applies to ineffective assistance of counsel claims rendered by non-attorneys); Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”). We therefore find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4509785 (BIA)

2010 WL 4509767 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUDY PINEDA
File: AXXX XX7 681 - Miami, FL
October 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandra Echevarria, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 29, 2009, denying his motion to reopen proceedings so that he may pursue his application for adjustment of status, pursuant to a Petition for Alien Relative (Form I-130) filed by his United States citizen wife. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility made by the Immigration Judge, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2010). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
A motion to reopen will not be granted unless evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available or could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent argues that his new evidence is the Form I-130 filed by his wife, as well as his application for adjustment of status (Form I-485) (Respondent’s Br. at 3). However, as the respondent himself concedes, these documents were filed with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) in 1997 (Respondent’s Br. at 3). He did not establish that this information was not available or could not have been presented at the hearing. Accordingly, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen.
 
The respondent argues that he has a “colorable claim” to ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge, because that attorney did not notify the Immigration Judge of the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status. However, the respondent chose not to comply with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance (Respondent’s Br. at 11-12; I.J. at 2). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
In his decision dated March 23, 2009, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent post-conclusion voluntary departure. The respondent requests that if the Board does not grant his motion to reopen, we alternatively reinstate the grant of voluntary departure, or order his bond returned to him. As the respondent acknowledges, a filing of a motion to reopen prior to the expiration of the period allowed for voluntary departure serves to automatically terminate the grant of voluntary departure. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1). Thus, we do not have discretion to reinstate voluntary departure. The previously-entered voluntary departure order will be vacated. Further, we do not have jurisdiction to order the return of the posted bond, as that is in the purview of the Department of Homeland Security. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(4).
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s order granting voluntary departure, dated March 23, 2009, is vacated, and the alternative order of removal is in effect.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE REFUJIO SILVA-TURRUBIARTES
