2010 WL 4035455 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOSES MACHARIA GICHUHI
File: AXXX XX2 604 - Baltimore, MD
September 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stuart J. Snyder, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on June 3, 2009, after failing to appear at a hearing on that date. He filed a motion to reopen on July 22, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied it on September 25, 2009, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be sustained.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide this appeal de novo.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that “exceptional circumstances” prevented his appearance at the hearing. We consider the totality of the evidence in evaluating this issue. See Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) and Matter of B-A-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1998). See also generally Kaweesa v. Gonzales, 450 F.3d 62, 68-69 (1st Cir. 2006) (including the strength of the alien’s underlying claim); Romero-Morales v. INS, 25 F.3d 125, 129-31 (2d Cir. 1994); Celis-Castellano v. Ashcroft, 298 F.3d 888 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
Here, we consider that the record contains a statement from the respondent’s former attorney. Therein, counsel admits that she erroneously instructed the respondent to appear for his hearing on June 9, 2009, rather than the correct date of June 3, 2009. The respondent’s declaration corroborates his attorney’s account. In addition, we consider that the respondent filed his motion to reopen almost immediately after learning that he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. His due diligence in this matter is strong evidence of the respondent’s lack of intention to miss his hearing. We also consider that the respondent appeared at numerous previous hearings since 2007. Further, a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel is present and the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See generally Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). It appears that the respondent’s failure to appear at his hearing was caused by an errant instruction from his former counsel.
 
*2 We find based on the totality of the evidence that the respondent has established that reopening of these proceedings based on “exceptional circumstances” is appropriate. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and these proceedings are reopened and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035455 (BIA)

2010 WL 4035459 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PATRICK LEVILAIN
File: AXXX XX0 652 - Portland, OR
September 23, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah C. Schreck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Canada, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s November 5, 2009, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed to Canada. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition brief. The appeal will be dismissed. While this matter was on appeal, the respondent filed what the Board construes to be a motion to remand. This motion will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of H-L-H- & Z-Y-Z-, 25 I&N Dec. 209, 211 (BIA 2010).
 
The respondent, a citizen and native of Canada, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 1967 (Exh. 1; I.J at 1). On February 14, 2007, in the Circuit Court of Marion County, State of Oregon, he pled guilty to the offense of burglary in the first degree, in violation of section 164.225 of the Oregon Revised Statutes, and he was sentenced to 19 months confinement (Exh. 2, Tab B at 1; I.J. at 1). In his decision, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent was convicted of a theft or burglary offense for which a term of imprisonment of at least 1 year was imposed, thus constituting an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G).
 
The respondent argues that his criminal conviction was invalid because he received ineffective assistance from a prior criminal counsel, who allegedly failed to inform him of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea (Respondent’s Notice of Appeal at 4). Although the respondent is apparently appealing a Circuit Court denial of post-conviction relief to the Supreme Court of the State of Oregon, to date the record does not reflect that the conviction has actually been overturned. Thus, his pending collateral attack on his conviction does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes, or warrant a continuance. Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
In a letter associated with the record in April 2010, the respondent’s former immigration counsel appeared to reference Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct. 1473, 2010 WL 1222274 (March 31, 2010), in which the United States Supreme Court ruled that a lawyer representing an alien in connection with a guilty plea to a criminal offense has a constitutional duty to advise the alien about the risk of deportation arising from the conviction. However, any suggestion that the respondent’s conviction might be invalid in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change its finality for immigration purposes of such conviction, unless and until it is in fact overturned or materially amended. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent alleged that he was ill served by the failure of his prior immigration counsel to seek cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, and he requested a remand so that the Immigration Judge could consider this application in the first instance (Respondent’s Br. at 1-2, 9). We construe the respondent’s submissions in this regard as a motion to remand and reopen his proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4).
 
However, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. That is, he has not submitted an affidavit detailing the agreement he had with his prior counsel, asserted that the prior counsel had been informed of any allegations leveled against him and been given an opportunity to respond, or confirmed that disciplinary complaints had been filed against the prior counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). His actions to date could not even be construed as substantial compliance. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 825-26 (9th Cir. 2003) (recognizing substantial compliance with Lozada procedural requirements where legitimacy of the claim is plain on the face of the administrative record).
 
Finally, a motion to reopen will not be granted unless it establishes a prima facie case of statutory and discretionary eligibility for the underlying substantive relief sought. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Since the respondent’s aggravated felony conviction thus far remains intact, he has not shown that he is statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. Therefore, he has not established that he was prejudiced by the apparent failure of his prior immigration counsel to file an application for cancellation of removal. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); Ahmed v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 768, 772 (9th Cir. 2008).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035459 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 4035467 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS MOREL A.K.A. JOSE MOREL A.K.A. JOSE MORELL
File: AXXX XX7 187 - Raymondville, TX
September 22, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jorge Guttlein, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Termination, reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic and a lawful permanent resident of the United States since his admission as an immigrant on or about November 27, 1991, has filed a timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s May 10, 2010, decision. In that decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable, as charged, based on his admissions (Tr. at 5-6), and concessions of removability (Tr. at 6), and statutorily ineligible for relief from removal, including cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3), on account of the respondent’s 2009 New York conviction for Criminal Sale of Marijuana in the fourth degree, in violation of NY PENAL LAW § 221.40. In addition, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate based on the respondent’s contention that the order by a New York court granting the respondent’s motion to file an untimely notice of appeal undermined the finality of the controlled substance conviction underlying these proceedings. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal in forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984).
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in an appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008) rev’d on other grounds, Kaplun v. Attorney General of the United States, 602 F.3d 260 (3d Cir. 2010); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
*2 At the outset, we address the respondent’s motion, styled as a motion to reopen and reconsider, that was filed contemporaneously with his Notice of Appeal (form EOIR-26), alleging due process violations in the Immigration Judge’s conduct of these proceedings. We have held an alien must demonstrate that he has been prejudiced by a violation of a procedural rule or regulation before his deportation proceeding will be invalidated. Matter of Garcia-Flores, 17 I&N Dec. 325, 329 (BIA 1980). This is consistent with the rule that an alien must have been prejudiced by a procedural defect in his deportation proceeding before he will be found to have suffered a denial of due process. See Ka Fung Chan v. INS, 634 F.2d 248, 258 (5th Cir. 1981). In the matter before us, no such prejudice has been shown. Moreover, the record reflects that the respondent had ample opportunity to prepare for and address the removal charges, and eligibility for relief from removal.
 
Although the respondent declined to accept the Immigration Judge’s offer to continue the proceedings in order to secure legal representation (Tr. at 5), the Immigration Judge nevertheless continued the proceedings on several occasions for that purpose (Tr. at 7-8, 9-11).¿FN1º Nonetheless, the record reflects that the respondent freely admitted that he had suffered a 2009 New York conviction for Criminal Sale of Marijuana in the Fourth Degree, in violation of NY PENAL LAW § 221.40, as alleged in the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) (form I-862) (Exh. 1) (Tr. at 5-6). In addition, the respondent conceded that as a result of this 2009 New York drug-trafficking conviction, he was also subject to removability as charged in the NTA (Tr. at 6). Thus, notwithstanding the respondent’s appellate contentions, we consider that the respondent’s removability is not at issue. The distinct and formal admissions, made by respondent during his removal hearing, are binding on the respondent as judicial admissions, and he will not now be heard to challenge the factual findings or legal conclusions of the Immigration Judge that arose from those admissions. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c). See also Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(B) (authorizing summary dismissal of appeals when “¿tºhe only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior proceeding”); see generally Pool Co. v. Cooper, 274 F.3d 173, 185 (5th Cir. 2001) (finding that the court was entitled to treat a concession as a binding judicial admission of fact.); Martinez v. Bally’s Louisiana, Inc., 244 F.3d 474 (5th Cir. 2001) (a judicial admission is conclusive, unless the court allows it to be withdrawn).
 
*3 Moreover, like the Immigration Judge, we conclude that the respondent’s 2009 New York controlled substance offense is an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B). According to the United States Supreme Court, a state drug offense qualifies as a drug trafficking crime under 18 U.S.C. § 924(c) and, by extension, an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act if the offense would have been punishable as a felony under the Federal Controlled Substances Act (“CSA”), 21 U.S.C. § 802 et seq. See Lopez v. Gonzales, 127 S. Ct. 625, 630-31 (2006). The New York offense of criminal sale of marijuana is comparable to the federal offense of marijuana distribution, which is punishable under 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D) by a term of imprisonment of up to 5 years, making it a federal “felony.”¿FN2º Id., at 631 n.7 (holding that “for purposes of § 924(c)(2) the crimes the CSA defines as ‘felonies’ are those crimes to which it assigns a punishment exceeding one year’s imprisonment.”); see also Matter of Aruna, 24 I&N Dec. 452, 456-57 (BIA 2008) (citing United States v. Walker, 302 F.3d 322, 324-25 (5th Cir. 2002)); compare NY PENAL LAW § 221.35 (criminalizing the sale of 2 grams or less of marijuana for “no consideration”). Although the record does not show whether this “sale” by the respondent included an exchange of funds or were non-remunerative transfers, this fact does not benefit the respondent because all such transfers are encompassed within the meaning of the term “distribute” as employed in 21 U.S.C. § 84(a)(1). See 21 U.S.C. §§ 802(8), (11); see also United States v. Workopich, 479 F.2d 1142, 1147 n.6 (5th Cir. 1973). Accordingly, the respondent’s 2009 New York State conviction for criminal sale of marijuana would be punishable as a federal felony even if the conviction record did not reflect that he transferred marijuana in exchange for money or other consideration.
 
Furthermore, contrary to the respondent’s appellate contention, his 2009 New York controlled substance conviction is not under direct appeal. Rather, according to the documentation he has presented accompanying the appeal, he has been granted permission by the New York criminal court to file an untimely notice of appeal in his criminal case alleging ineffective assistance from criminal counsel. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at Exh. C. However, the respondent has submitted no evidence that any post-conviction document has been filed pursuant to that order. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at Exh. E.
 
*4 Thus, to the extent that the respondent’s arguments are in the nature of collateral attacks on his conviction, they are not properly before us for consideration, as neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board can entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996); Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999). Further, the fact that the respondent may be pursuing post-conviction relief in the form of a collateral attack on a conviction in criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506 (BIA 1992). The respondent had (and has) a valid “conviction” for immigration purposes, and his collateral attack upon that conviction does not operate to negate its validity until it is in fact overturned or materially amended. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, supra, at 327; see also Singh v. Holder, 568 F.3d 525, 528 (5th Cir. 2009) (citing Zinnanti v. INS, 651 F.2d 420, 421 (5th Cir. 1981) (“Once the ¿state criminalº conviction becomes final, it provides a valid basis for deportation unless it is overturned in a judicial post-conviction proceeding.”).
 
The respondent has presented no new evidence on appeal to indicate that his 2009 New York controlled substance conviction underlying these removal proceedings, has been overturned, vacated, or expunged; rather, the evidence merely indicates that the respondent has been granted permission by the New York court to file an untimely notice of appeal of that conviction, which has yet to be perfected. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at Exh. E.
 
We acknowledge the Supreme Court’s recent decision of Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), in which the court ruled that a lawyer representing an alien in connection with a guilty plea to a criminal offense has a constitutional duty to advise the alien about the risk of deportation arising from the conviction. Notwithstanding, any speculation that the respondent’s 2009 New York controlled substance conviction might be invalid on this basis, including in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change the finality for immigration purposes of such conviction, unless and until it were to be overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996).
 
Thus, we are not persuaded to disturb the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is removable based on his admissions (Tr. at 5-6) and concession of removability (Tr. at 6). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c). Moreover, having expressed no fear of persecution or torture if returned to the Dominican Republic (Tr. at 6), the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal, including cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. See section 240(c)(4)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d).
 
*5 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent, in his motion, raises allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, the record fails to disclose that the respondent was ever represented by legal counsel during the course of the proceedings before the Immigration Judge. We note, parenthetically, the respondent is currently represented by legal counsel on appeal, and the allegations raised in the motion are not directed at present counsel.

2

In this regard, we find the respondent’s reliance on the decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, in Smith v. Gonzales, 468 F.3d 272 (5th Cir. 2006), to be misplaced. In that case, which was decided prior to the Supreme Court’s decision in Lopez v. Gonzales, the court found that the New York offense of Criminal Sale of Marijuana in the Fourth Degree, in violation of NY PENAL LAW § 221.40, did not qualify as a drug trafficking crime that could be punishable as a felony under the CSA, based on the recidivist sentencing enhancement provision found in 21 U.S.C. § 844(a). Id. at 276-77. Here, however, we find the analogous federal felony statute under the CSA to be marijuana distribution, which is punishable under 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D). Thus, the “aggravated felony” felony issue based on recidivist possession, addressed by the Fifth Circuit in Smith v. Gonzales, supra, is not controlling.
